<h2 class="calibre_4" id="calibre_pb_3" aid="2RHM2"><span class="calibre5" aid="2RHM3"><a class="calibre6" href="kindle:pos:fid:002R:off:000000005A" id="filepos9281" aid="2RHM4"><span class="bold1" aid="2RHM5">ILLUSTRATIONS</span></a></span></h2><div class="calibre_5" aid="2RHM6"> </div>
<div class="calibre_1" aid="2RHM7"> </div><p class="calibre_6" aid="2RHM8"><span class="calibre3" aid="2RHM9">SILENCE CAME BACK IN, FILLING JAGGED SPACES<br class="calibre2"/>(THE GUNSLINGER)</span></p><p class="calibre_7" aid="2RHMA"><span class="calibre3" aid="2RHMB"><span class="italic" aid="2RHMC">facing page</span>
<a href="kindle:pos:fid:0011:off:0000000583" aid="2RHMD">ref-1, ref-2</a></span></p>
<div class="calibre_1" aid="2RHME"> </div><p class="calibre_6" aid="2RHMF"><span class="calibre3" aid="2RHMG">THEY PAUSED . . . LOOKING UP AT THE DANGLING,<br class="calibre2"/>TWISTING BODY (THE WAY STATION)</span></p><p class="calibre_7" aid="2RHMH"><span class="calibre3" aid="2RHMI"><span class="italic" aid="2RHMJ">facing page</span>

<div class="calibre2" aid="3Q281">

<h2 class="calibre_4" id="calibre_pb_5" aid="3Q282"><span class="calibre5" aid="3Q283"><a class="calibre6" id="filepos10982" aid="3Q284"></a><a class="calibre6" href="kindle:pos:fid:002R:off:00000000D6" id="filepos10989" aid="3Q285"><span class="bold1" aid="3Q286">INTRODUCTION<br class="calibre7"/><br class="calibre7"/><span class="calibre6" aid="3Q287"><span class="calibre8" aid="3Q288">On Being Nineteen</span></span><br class="calibre7"/>(<span class="italic1" aid="3Q289">and a Few Other Things</span>)</span>
</a></span></h2><div class="calibre_5" aid="3Q28A"> </div>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="3Q28B"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="3Q28C"><span class="calibre9" aid="3Q28D"><span class="bold1" aid="3Q28E">I</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="3Q28F"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="3Q28G"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q28H">Hobbits were big when I was nineteen (a number of some import in the stories you are about to read).</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q28I"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q28J">There were probably half a dozen Merrys and Pippins slogging through the mud at Max Yasgur’s farm during the Great Woodstock Music Festival, twice as many Frodos, and hippie Gandalfs without number. J.R.R. Tolkien’s <span class="italic" aid="3Q28K">The Lord of the Rings</span> was madly popular in those days, and while I never made it to Woodstock (say sorry), I suppose I was at least a halfling-hippie. Enough of one, at any rate, to have read the books and fallen in love with them. The <span class="italic" aid="3Q28L">Dark Tower</span> books, like most long fantasy tales written by men and women of my generation (<span class="italic" aid="3Q28M">The Chronicles of Thomas Covenant</span>, by Stephen Donaldson, and <span class="italic" aid="3Q28N">The Sword of Shannara</span>, by Terry Brooks, are just two of many), were born out of Tolkien’s.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q28O"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q28P">But although I read the books in 1966 and 1967, I held off writing. I responded (and with rather touching wholeheartedness) to the sweep of Tolkien’s imagination—to the ambition of his story—but I wanted to write my own kind of story, and had I started then, I would have written his. That, as the late Tricky Dick Nixon was fond of saying, would have been wrong. Thanks <a aid="3Q28Q"></a>to Mr. Tolkien, the twentieth century had all the elves and wizards it needed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q28R"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q28S">In 1967, I didn’t have any idea what my kind of story might be, but that didn’t matter; I felt positive I’d know it when it passed me on the street. I was nineteen and arrogant. Certainly arrogant enough to feel I could wait a little while on my muse and my masterpiece (as I was sure it would be). At nineteen, it seems to me, one has a right to be arrogant; time has usually not begun its stealthy and rotten subtractions. It takes away your hair and your jump-shot, according to a popular country song, but in truth it takes away a lot more than that. I didn’t know it in 1966 and ’67, and if I had, I wouldn’t have cared. I could imagine—barely—being forty, but fifty? No. <span class="italic" aid="3Q28T">Sixty?</span> Never! Sixty was out of the question. And at nineteen, that’s just the way to be. Nineteen is the age where you say <span class="italic" aid="3Q28U">Look out, world, I’m smokin’TNT and I’m drinkin’ dynamite, so if you know what’s good for ya, get out of my way—here comes Stevie</span>.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q28V"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q290">Nineteen’s a selfish age and finds one’s cares tightly circumscribed. I had a lot of reach, and I cared about that. I had a lot of ambition, and I cared about that. I had a typewriter that I carried from one shithole apartment to the next, always with a deck of smokes in my pocket and a smile on my face. The compromises of middle age were distant, the insults of old age over the horizon. Like the protagonist in that Bob Seger song they now use to sell the trucks, I felt endlessly powerful and endlessly optimistic; my pockets were empty, but my head was full of things I wanted to say and my heart was full of stories I wanted to tell. Sounds corny now; felt wonderful then. Felt very cool. More than anything else I wanted to get inside my readers’ defenses, wanted to rip them and ravish them and change them forever with nothing but story. And I felt I could do those things. I felt I had been <span class="italic" aid="3Q291">made</span> to do those things.</span></p>
<a aid="3Q292"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q293"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q294">How conceited does that sound? A lot or a little? Either way, I don’t apologize. I was nineteen. There was not so much as a strand of gray in my beard. I had three pairs of jeans, one pair of boots, the idea that the world was my oyster, and nothing that happened in the next twenty years proved me wrong. Then, around the age of thirty-nine, my troubles set in: drink, drugs, a road accident that changed the way I walked (among other things). I’ve written about them at length and need not write about them here. Besides, it’s the same for you, right? The world eventually sends out a mean-ass Patrol Boy to slow your progress and show you who’s boss. You reading this have undoubtedly met yours (or will); I met mine, and I’m sure he’ll be back. He’s got my address. He’s a mean guy, a Bad Lieutenant, the sworn enemy of goofery, fuckery, pride, ambition, loud music, and all things nineteen.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q295"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q296">But I still think that’s a pretty fine age. Maybe the best age. You can rock and roll all night, but when the music dies out and the beer wears off, you’re able to think. And dream big dreams. The mean Patrol Boy cuts you down to size eventually, and if you start out small, why, there’s almost nothing left but the cuffs of your pants when he’s done with you. “Got another one!” he shouts, and strides on with his citation book in his hand. So a little arrogance (or even a lot) isn’t such a bad thing, although your mother undoubtedly told you different. Mine did. <span class="italic" aid="3Q297">Pride goeth before a fall, Stephen</span>, she said . . . and then I found out—right around the age that is 19 x 2—that eventually you fall down, anyway. Or get pushed into the ditch. At nineteen they can card you in the bars and tell you to get the fuck out, put your sorry act (and sorrier ass) back on the street, but they can’t card you when you sit down to paint a picture, write a poem, or tell a story, by God, and if you reading this happen to be very young, don’t let your <a aid="3Q298"></a>elders and supposed betters tell you any different. Sure, you’ve never been to Paris. No, you never ran with the bulls at Pamplona. Yes, you’re a pissant who had no hair in your armpits until three years ago—but so what? If you don’t start out too big for your britches, how are you gonna fill ’em when you grow up? Let it rip regardless of what anybody tells you, that’s my idea; sit down and <span class="italic" aid="3Q299">smoke</span> that baby.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="3Q29A"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="3Q29B"><span class="calibre9" aid="3Q29C"><span class="bold1" aid="3Q29D">II</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="3Q29E"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="3Q29F"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q29G">I think novelists come in two types, and that includes the sort of fledgling novelist I was by 1970. Those who are bound for the more literary or “serious” side of the job examine every possible subject in light of this question: <span class="italic" aid="3Q29H">What would writing this sort of story mean to me?</span> Those whose destiny (or ka, if you like) is to include the writing of popular novels are apt to ask a very different one: <span class="italic" aid="3Q29I">What would writing this sort of story mean to others?</span> The “serious” novelist is looking for answers and keys to the self; the “popular” novelist is looking for an audience. Both kinds of writer are equally selfish. I’ve known a good many, and will set my watch and warrant upon it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q29J"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q29K">Anyway, I believe that even at the age of nineteen, I recognized the story of Frodo and his efforts to rid himself of the One Great Ring as one belonging to the second group. They were the adventures of an essentially British band of pilgrims set against a backdrop of vaguely Norse mythology. I liked the idea of the quest—<span class="italic" aid="3Q29L">loved</span> it, in fact—but I had no interest in either Tolkien’s sturdy peasant characters (that’s not to say I didn’t like them, because I did) or his bosky Scandinavian settings. If I tried going in that direction, I’d get it all wrong.</span></p>
<a aid="3Q29M"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q29N"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q29O">So I waited. By 1970 I was twenty-two, the first strands of gray had showed up in my beard (I think smoking two and a half packs of Pall Malls a day probably had something to do with that), but even at twenty-two, one can afford to wait. At twenty-two, time is still on one’s side, although even then that bad old Patrol Boy’s in the neighborhood and asking questions.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q29P"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q29Q">Then, in an almost completely empty movie theater (the Bijou, in Bangor, Maine, if it matters), I saw a film directed by Sergio Leone. It was called <span class="italic" aid="3Q29R">The Good, the Bad, and the Ugly,</span> and before the film was even half over, I realized that what I wanted to write was a novel that contained Tolkien’s sense of quest and magic but set against Leone’s almost absurdly majestic Western backdrop. If you’ve only seen this gonzo Western on your television screen, you don’t understand what I’m talking about—cry your pardon, but it’s true. On a movie screen, projected through the correct Panavision lenses, <span class="italic" aid="3Q29S">TG, TB, &amp; TU</span> is an epic to rival <span class="italic" aid="3Q29T">Ben-Hur</span>. Clint Eastwood appears roughly eighteen feet tall, with each wiry jut of stubble on his cheeks looking roughly the size of a young redwood tree. The grooves bracketing Lee Van Cleef’s mouth are as deep as canyons, and there could be a thinny (see <span class="italic" aid="3Q29U">Wizard and Glass</span>) at the bottom of each one. The desert settings appear to stretch at least out as far as the orbit of the planet Neptune. And the barrel of each gun looks to be roughly as large as the Holland Tunnel.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q29V"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2A0">What I wanted even more than the setting was that feeling of epic, apocalyptic <span class="italic" aid="3Q2A1">size</span>. The fact that Leone knew jack shit about American geography (according to one of the characters, Chicago is somewhere in the vicinity of Phoenix, Arizona) added to the film’s sense of magnificent dislocation. And in my enthusiasm—the sort only a young person can muster, I think—I wanted to write not just a <span class="italic" aid="3Q2A2">long</span> book, but <span class="italic" aid="3Q2A3">the longest popular novel in</span>
<a aid="3Q2A4"></a><span class="italic" aid="3Q2A5">history</span>. I did not succeed in doing that, but I feel I had a decent rip; <span class="italic" aid="3Q2A6">The Dark Tower,</span> volumes one through seven, really comprise a single tale, and the first four volumes run to just over two thousand pages in paperback. The final three volumes run another twenty-five hundred in manuscript. I’m not trying to imply here that length has anything whatsoever to do with quality; I’m just saying that I wanted to write an epic, and in some ways, I succeeded. If you were to ask me <span class="italic" aid="3Q2A7">why</span> I wanted to do that, I couldn’t tell you. Maybe it’s a part of growing up American: build the tallest, dig the deepest, write the longest. And that head-scratching puzzlement when the question of motivation comes up? Seems to me that that is also part of being an American. In the end we are reduced to saying <span class="italic" aid="3Q2A8">It seemed like a good idea at the time.</span></span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="3Q2A9"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="3Q2AA"><span class="calibre9" aid="3Q2AB"><span class="bold1" aid="3Q2AC">III</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="3Q2AD"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="3Q2AE"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2AF">Another thing about being nineteen, do it please ya: it is the age, I think, where a lot of us somehow get stuck (mentally and emotionally, if not physically). The years slide by and one day you find yourself looking into the mirror with real puzzlement. <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AG">Why are those lines on my face?</span> you wonder. <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AH">Where did that stupid potbelly come from? Hell, I’m only nineteen!</span> This is hardly an original concept, but that in no way subtracts from one’s amazement.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2AI"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2AJ">Time puts gray in your beard, time takes away your jump-shot, and all the while you’re thinking—silly you—that it’s still on your side. The logical side of you knows better, but your heart refuses to believe it. If you’re lucky, the Patrol Boy who cited you for going too fast and having too much fun also gives you a dose of smelling salts. That was more or less what happened to me <a aid="3Q2AK"></a>near the end of the twentieth century. It came in the form of a Plymouth van that knocked me into the ditch beside a road in my hometown.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2AL"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2AM">About three years after that accident I did a book signing for <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AN">From a Buick 8</span> at a Borders store in Dearborn, Michigan. When one guy got to the head of the line, he said he was really, really glad that I was still alive. (I get this a lot, and it beats the <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AO">shit</span> out of “Why the hell didn’t you die?”)</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2AP"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2AQ">“I was with this good friend of mine when we heard you got popped,” he said. “Man, we just started shaking our heads and saying ‘There goes the Tower, it’s tilting, it’s falling, ahhh, shit, he’ll <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AR">never</span> finish it now.’ ”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2AS"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2AT">A version of the same idea had occurred to me—the troubling idea that, having built the Dark Tower in the collective imagination of a million readers, I might have a responsibility to make it safe for as long as people wanted to read about it. That might be for only five years; for all I know, it might be five hundred. Fantasy stories, the bad as well as the good (even now, someone out there is probably reading <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AU">Varney the Vampire</span> or <span class="italic" aid="3Q2AV">The Monk</span>), seem to have long shelf lives. Roland’s way of protecting the Tower is to try to remove the threat to the Beams that hold the Tower up. I would have to do it, I realized after my accident, by finishing the gunslinger’s story.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2B0"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2B1">During the long pauses between the writing and publication of the first four <span class="italic" aid="3Q2B2">Dark Tower</span> tales, I received hundreds of “pack your bags, we’re going on a guilt trip” letters. In 1998 (when I was laboring under the mistaken impression that I was still basically nineteen, in other words), I got one from an “82-yr-old Gramma, don’t mean to Bother You w/My Troubles BUT!! very Sick These Days.” The Gramma told me she probably had only a year to live (“14 Mo’s at Outside, Cancer all thru Me”), and while <a aid="3Q2B3"></a>she didn’t expect me to finish Roland’s tale in that time just for her, she wanted to know if I couldn’t please (<span class="italic" aid="3Q2B4">please</span>) just tell her how it came out. The line that wrenched my heart (although not quite enough to start writing again) was her promise to “not tell a Single Soul.” A year later—probably after the accident that landed me in the hospital—one of my assistants, Marsha DiFilippo, got a letter from a fellow on death row in either Texas or Florida, wanting to know essentially the same thing: how does it come out? (He promised to take the secret to the grave with him, which gave me the creeps.)</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2B5"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2B6">I would have given both of these folks what they wanted—a summary of Roland’s further adventures—if I could have done, but alas, I couldn’t. I had no idea of how things were going to turn out with the gunslinger and his friends. To know, I have to write. I once had an outline, but I lost it along the way. (It probably wasn’t worth a tin shit, anyway.) All I had was a few notes (“<span class="underline" aid="3Q2B7">Chussit, chissit, chassit,</span> something-something-<span class="underline" aid="3Q2B8">basket</span>” reads one lying on the desk as I write this). Eventually, starting in July of 2001, I began to write again. I knew by then I was no longer nineteen, nor exempt from any of the ills to which the flesh is heir. I knew I was going to be sixty, maybe even seventy. And I wanted to finish my story before the bad Patrol Boy came for the last time. I had no urge to be filed away with <span class="italic" aid="3Q2B9">The Canterbury Tales</span> and <span class="italic" aid="3Q2BA">The Mystery of Edwin Drood</span>.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2BB"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2BC">The result—for better or worse—lies before you, Constant Reader, whether you reading this are starting with Volume One or are preparing for Volume Five. Like it or hate it, the story of Roland is now done. I hope you enjoy it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="3Q2BD"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2BE">As for me, I had the time of my life.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_7" aid="3Q2BF"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2BG">Stephen King</span></p><p class="calibre_7" aid="3Q2BH"><span class="calibre3" aid="3Q2BI">January 25, 2003</span></p>
<a aid="3Q2BJ"></a>
<div class="mbp_pagebreak" id="calibre_pb_6" aid="3Q2BK"></div>
<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>
<html xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">
  <head>
    <title>The Gunslinger</title>
    <meta content="text/html; charset=utf-8" http-equiv="Content-Type"/>
  <link href="kindle:flow:0001?mime=text/css" rel="stylesheet" type="text/css"/>
<link href="kindle:flow:0002?mime=text/css" rel="stylesheet" type="text/css"/>
</head>
  <body class="calibre" aid="4OIQ0">
<div class="calibre2" aid="4OIQ1">
</div>
</body>
</html>
<h2 class="calibre_4" id="calibre_pb_7" aid="4OIQ2"><span class="calibre5" aid="4OIQ3"><a class="calibre6" href="kindle:pos:fid:002R:off:00000000OE" id="filepos28253" aid="4OIQ4"><span class="calibre6" aid="4OIQ5"><span class="calibre8" aid="4OIQ6"><span class="bold1" aid="4OIQ7">Foreword</span></span></span></a></span></h2><div class="calibre_5" aid="4OIQ8"> </div>
<p class="calibre10" aid="4OIQ9"> </p><p class="calibre10" aid="4OIQA"> </p><p class="calibre10" aid="4OIQB"> </p>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="4OIQC"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIQD">Most of what writers write about their work is ill-informed bullshit.<a href="kindle:pos:fid:002O:off:0000000024" id="filepos28693" aid="4OIQE">*</a> That is why you have never seen a book entitled <span class="italic" aid="4OIQF">One Hundred Great Introductions of Western Civilization</span> or <span class="italic" aid="4OIQG">Best-Loved Forewords of the American People</span>. This is a judgment call on my part, of course, but after writing at least fifty introductions and forewords—not to mention an entire book about the craft of fiction—I think it’s one I have a right to make. And I think you can take me seriously when I tell you this might be one of those rare occasions upon which I actually have something worth saying.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIQH"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIQI">A few years ago, I created some furor among my readers by offering a revised and expanded version of my novel <span class="italic" aid="4OIQJ">The Stand</span>. I was justifiably nervous about that book, because <span class="italic" aid="4OIQK">The Stand</span> has always been the novel my readers have loved the best (as far as the most passionate of the “<span class="italic" aid="4OIQL">Stand</span>-fans” are concerned, I could have died in 1980 without making the world a noticeably poorer place).</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIQM"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIQN">If there is a story that rivals <span class="italic" aid="4OIQO">The Stand</span> in the imagination of <a aid="4OIQP"></a>King readers, it’s probably the tale of Roland Deschain and his search for the Dark Tower. And now—goddamn!—I’ve gone and done the same thing again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIQQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIQR">Except I haven’t, not really, and I want you to know it. I also want you to know what I <span class="italic" aid="4OIQS">have</span> done, and why. It may not be important to you, but it’s <span class="italic" aid="4OIQT">very</span> important to me, and thus this foreword is exempt (I hope) from King’s Bullshit Rule.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIQU"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIQV">First, please be reminded that <span class="italic" aid="4OIR0">The Stand</span> sustained deep cuts in manuscript not for editorial reasons but for financial ones. (There were binding limitations, too, but I don’t even want to go there.) What I reinstated in the late eighties were revised sections of preexisting manuscript. I also revised the work as a whole, mostly to acknowledge the AIDS epidemic, which blossomed (if that is the word) between the first issue of <span class="italic" aid="4OIR1">The Stand</span> and the publication of the revised version eight or nine years later. The result was a volume about 100,000 words longer than the original.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIR2"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIR3">In the case of <span class="italic" aid="4OIR4">The Gunslinger</span>, the original volume was slim, and the added material in this version amounts to a mere thirty-five pages, or about nine thousand words. If you have read <span class="italic" aid="4OIR5">The Gunslinger</span> before, you’ll only find two or three totally new scenes here. Dark Tower purists (of which there are a surprising number—just check the Web) will want to read the book again, of course, and most of them are apt to do so with a mixture of curiosity and irritation. I sympathize, but must say I’m less concerned with them than with readers who have never encountered Roland and his ka-tet.<a href="kindle:pos:fid:002P:off:0000000024" id="filepos31780" aid="4OIR6">*</a></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIR7"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIR8">In spite of its fervent followers, the tale of the Tower is far less known by my readers than is <span class="italic" aid="4OIR9">The Stand</span>. Sometimes, when I <a aid="4OIRA"></a>do readings, I’ll ask those present to raise their hands if they’ve read one or more of my novels. Since they’ve bothered to come at all—sometimes going to the added inconvenience of hiring a baby-sitter and incurring the added expense of gassing up the old sedan—it comes as no surprise that most of them raise their hands. Then I’ll ask them to keep their hands up if they’ve read one or more of the Dark Tower stories. When I do that, at least half the hands in the hall invariably go down. The conclusion is clear enough: although I’ve spent an inordinate amount of time writing these books in the thirty-three years between 1970 and 2003, comparatively few people have read them. Yet those who have are passionate about them, and I’m fairly passionate myself—enough so, in any case, that I was never able to let Roland creep away into that exile which is the unhappy home of unfulfilled characters (think of Chaucer’s pilgrims on the way to Canterbury, or the people who populate Charles Dickens’s unfinished final novel, <span class="italic" aid="4OIRB">The Mystery of Edwin Drood</span>).</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIRC"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIRD">I think that I’d always assumed (somewhere in the back of my mind, for I cannot ever remember thinking about this consciously) that there would be time to finish, that perhaps God would even send me a singing telegram at the appointed hour: “Deedle-dum, deedle-dower/Get back to work, Stephen,/Finish the Tower.” And in a way, something like that really did happen, although it wasn’t a singing telegram but a close encounter with a Plymouth minivan that got me going again. If the vehicle that struck me that day had been a little bigger, or if the hit had been just a little squarer, it would have been a case of mourners please omit flowers, the King family thanks you for your sympathy. And Roland’s quest would have remained forever unfinished, at least by me.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIRE"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIRF">In any case, in 2001—by which time I’d begun to feel more <a aid="4OIRG"></a>myself again—I decided the time had come to finish Roland’s story. I pushed everything else aside and set to work on the final three books. As always, I did this not so much for the readers who demanded it as for myself.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIRH"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIRI">Although the revisions of the last two volumes still remain to be done as I write this in the winter of 2003, the books themselves were finished last summer. And, in the hiatus between the editorial work on Volume Five (<span class="italic" aid="4OIRJ">Wolves of the Calla</span>) and Volume Six (<span class="italic" aid="4OIRK">Song of Susannah</span>), I decided the time had come to go back to the beginning and start the final overall revisions. Why? Because these seven volumes were never really separate stories at all, but sections of a single long novel called <span class="italic" aid="4OIRL">The Dark Tower</span>, and the beginning was out of sync with the ending.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIRM"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIRN">My approach to revision hasn’t changed much over the years. I know there are writers who do it as they go along, but my method of attack has always been to plunge in and go as fast as I can, keeping the edge of my narrative blade as sharp as possible by constant use, and trying to outrun the novelist’s most insidious enemy, which is doubt. Looking back prompts too many questions: How believable are my characters? How interesting is my story? How good is this, really? Will anyone care? Do I care myself?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIRO"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIRP">When my first draft of a novel is done, I put it away, warts and all, to mellow. Some period of time later—six months, a year, two years, it doesn’t really matter—I can come back to it with a cooler (but still loving) eye, and begin the task of revising. And although each book of the Tower series was revised as a separate entity, I never really looked at the work as a whole until I’d finished Volume Seven, <span class="italic" aid="4OIRQ">The Dark Tower</span>.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIRR"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIRS">When I looked back at the first volume, which you now hold in your hands, three obvious truths presented themselves. The <a aid="4OIRT"></a>first was that <span class="italic" aid="4OIRU">The Gunslinger</span> had been written by a very young man, and had all the problems of a very young man’s book. The second was that it contained a great many errors and false starts, particularly in light of the volumes that followed.<a href="kindle:pos:fid:002Q:off:0000000024" id="filepos36632" aid="4OIRV">*</a> The third was that <span class="italic" aid="4OIS0">The Gunslinger</span> did not even <span class="italic" aid="4OIS1">sound</span> like the later books—it was, frankly, rather difficult to read. All too often I heard myself apologizing for it, and telling people that if they persevered, they would find the story really found its voice in <span class="italic" aid="4OIS2">The Drawing of the Three</span>.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIS3"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIS4">At one point in <span class="italic" aid="4OIS5">The Gunslinger</span>, Roland is described as the sort of man who would straighten pictures in strange hotel rooms. I’m that sort of guy myself, and to some extent, that is all that rewriting amounts to: straightening the pictures, vacuuming the floors, scrubbing the toilets. I did a great deal of housework in the course of this revision, and have had a chance to do what any writer wants to do with a work that is finished but still needs a final polish and tune-up: <span class="italic" aid="4OIS6">just make it right.</span> Once you know how things come out, you owe it to the potential reader—and to yourself—to go back and put things in order. That is what I have tried to do here, always being careful that no addition or change should give away the secrets hidden in the last three books of the cycle, secrets I have been patiently keeping for as long as thirty years in some cases.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OIS7"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OIS8">Before I close, I should say a word about the younger man who dared to write this book. That young man had been exposed to far too many writing seminars, and had grown far too used to the ideas those seminars promulgate: that one is writing for other <a aid="4OIS9"></a>people rather than one’s self; that language is more important than story; that ambiguity is to be preferred over clarity and simplicity, which are usually signs of a thick and literal mind. As a result, I was not surprised to find a high degree of pretension in Roland’s debut appearance (not to mention what seemed like thousands of unnecessary adverbs). I removed as much of this hollow blather as I could, and do not regret a single cut made in that regard. In other places—invariably those where I’d been seduced into forgetting the writing seminar ideas by some particularly entrancing piece of story—I was able to let the writing almost entirely alone, save for the usual bits of revision any writer needs to do. As I have pointed out in another context, only God gets it right the first time.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OISA"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OISB">In any case, I didn’t want to muzzle or even really change the way this story is told; for all its faults, it has its own special charms, it seems to me. To change it too completely would have been to repudiate the person who first wrote of the gunslinger in the late spring and early summer of 1970, and that I did not want to do.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="4OISC"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OISD">What I <span class="italic" aid="4OISE">did</span> want to do—and before the final volumes of the series came out, if possible—was to give newcomers to the tale of the Tower (and old readers who want to refresh their memories) a clearer start and a slightly easier entry into Roland’s world. I also wanted them to have a volume that more effectively foreshadowed coming events. I hope I have done that. And if you are one of those who have never visited the strange world through which Roland and his friends move, I hope you will enjoy the marvels you find there. More than anything else, I wanted to tell a tale of wonder. If you find yourself falling under the spell of the Dark Tower, even a little bit, I reckon I will have done my job, which was begun in 1970 and largely finished in 2003. Yet Roland <a aid="4OISF"></a>would be the first to point out that such a span of time means very little. In fact, when one quests for the Dark Tower, time is a matter of no concern at all.</span></p>
<p class="calibre10" aid="4OISG"> </p>
<p class="calibre_7" aid="4OISH"><span class="calibre3" aid="4OISI">—February 6, 2003</span></p>
<a aid="4OISJ"></a><a aid="4OISK"></a>
<div class="mbp_pagebreak" id="calibre_pb_8" aid="4OISL"></div>
<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>
<html xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">
  <head>
    <title>The Gunslinger</title>
    <meta content="text/html; charset=utf-8" http-equiv="Content-Type"/>
  <link href="kindle:flow:0001?mime=text/css" rel="stylesheet" type="text/css"/>
<link href="kindle:flow:0002?mime=text/css" rel="stylesheet" type="text/css"/>
</head>
  <body class="calibre" aid="5N3C0">
</body>
</html>
<div class="calibre2" aid="5N3C1">
<div class="calibre_2" aid="5N3C2"> </div><p class="calibre_9" aid="5N3C3"><span class="calibre3" aid="5N3C4">. . .  a stone, a leaf, an unfound door; of a leaf, a stone, a door. And of all the forgotten faces.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="5N3C5"><span class="calibre3" aid="5N3C6">Naked and alone we came into exile. In her dark womb, we did not know our mother’s face; from the prison of her flesh have we come into the unspeakable and incommunicable prison of this earth.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="5N3C7"><span class="calibre3" aid="5N3C8">Which of us has known his brother? Which of us has looked into his father’s heart? Which of us has not remained forever prison-pent? Which of us is not forever a stranger and alone?</span></p>
<p class="calibre10" aid="5N3C9"> </p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="5N3CA"><span class="calibre3" aid="5N3CB">. . .  O lost, and by the wind grieved, ghost, come back again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_7" aid="5N3CC"><span class="calibre3" aid="5N3CD">Thomas Wolfe</span></p>
<p class="calibre_7" aid="5N3CE"><span class="calibre3" aid="5N3CF"><span class="italic" aid="5N3CG">Look Homeward, Angel</span></span></p>
<a aid="5N3CH"></a>
<div class="mbp_pagebreak" id="calibre_pb_9" aid="5N3CI"></div>
</div>

<div class="calibre2" aid="8IL21">
<h1 class="calibre_12" id="calibre_pb_12" aid="8IL22"><span class="calibre11" aid="8IL23"><a class="calibre6" href="kindle:pos:fid:002R:off:000000010T" id="filepos42256" aid="8IL24"><span class="bold1" aid="8IL25">The</span><br class="calibre7"/>
<span class="calibre6" aid="8IL26"><span class="calibre12" aid="8IL27"><span class="bold1" aid="8IL28">GUNSLINGER</span></span></span></a></span></h1><p class="calibre10" aid="8IL29"> </p>
<p class="calibre_" aid="8IL2A"><img alt="thegunsling_31_0.jpg" class="calibre13" src="kindle:embed:000F?mime=image/jpeg"/></p>
<a aid="8IL2B"></a><a aid="8IL2C"></a>
<div class="mbp_pagebreak" id="calibre_pb_13" aid="8IL2D"></div>
</div>
<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>
<html xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">
  <head>
    <title>The Gunslinger</title>
    <meta content="text/html; charset=utf-8" http-equiv="Content-Type"/>
  <link href="kindle:flow:0001?mime=text/css" rel="stylesheet" type="text/css"/>
<link href="kindle:flow:0002?mime=text/css" rel="stylesheet" type="text/css"/>
</head>
  <body class="calibre" aid="9H5K0">
<div class="calibre2" aid="9H5K1">
</div>
</body>
</html>
<h2 class="calibre_4" id="calibre_pb_15" aid="9H5K2"><span class="calibre5" aid="9H5K3"><a class="calibre6" href="kindle:pos:fid:002R:off:00000001AP" id="filepos42638" aid="9H5K4"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5K5">CHAPTER ONE</span><br class="calibre7"/><br class="calibre7"/><span class="calibre6" aid="9H5K6"><span class="calibre15" aid="9H5K7"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5K8">The Gunslinger</span></span></span></a></span></h2><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H5K9"> </div>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H5KA"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H5KB"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H5KC"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5KD">I</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H5KE"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H5KF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KG">The man in black fled across the desert, and the gunslinger followed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5KH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KI">The desert was the apotheosis of all deserts, huge, standing to the sky for what looked like eternity in all directions. It was white and blinding and waterless and without feature save for the faint, cloudy haze of the mountains which sketched themselves on the horizon and the devil-grass which brought sweet dreams, nightmares, death. An occasional tombstone sign pointed the way, for once the drifted track that cut its way through the thick crust of alkali had been a highway. Coaches and buckas had followed it. The world had moved on since then. The world had emptied.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5KJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KK">The gunslinger had been struck by a momentary dizziness, a kind of yawing sensation that made the entire world seem ephemeral, almost a thing that could be looked through. It passed and, like the world upon whose hide he walked, he moved on. He passed the miles stolidly, not hurrying, not loafing. A hide waterbag was slung around his middle like a bloated sausage. It was almost full. He had progressed through the <span class="italic" aid="9H5KL">khef</span> over many years, and had reached perhaps the fifth level. Had he been a Manni holy man, he might not have even been thirsty; he could have <a aid="9H5KM"></a>watched his own body dehydrate with clinical, detached attention, watering its crevices and dark inner hollows only when his logic told him it must be done. He was not a Manni, however, nor a follower of the Man Jesus, and considered himself in no way holy. He was just an ordinary pilgrim, in other words, and all he could say with real certainty was that he was thirsty. And even so, he had no particular urge to drink. In a vague way, all this pleased him. It was what the country required, it was a thirsty country, and he had in his long life been nothing if not adaptable.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5KN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KO">Below the waterbag were his guns, carefully weighted to his hands; a plate had been added to each when they had come to him from his father, who had been lighter and not so tall. The two belts crisscrossed above his crotch. The holsters were oiled too deeply for even this Philistine sun to crack. The stocks of the guns were sandalwood, yellow and finely grained. Rawhide tie-downs held the holsters loosely to his thighs, and they swung a bit with his step; they had rubbed away the bluing of his jeans (and thinned the cloth) in a pair of arcs that looked almost like smiles. The brass casings of the cartridges looped into the gunbelts heliographed in the sun. There were fewer now. The leather made subtle creaking noises.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5KP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KQ">His shirt, the no-color of rain or dust, was open at the throat, with a rawhide thong dangling loosely in hand-punched eyelets. His hat was gone. So was the horn he had once carried; gone for years, that horn, spilled from the hand of a dying friend, and he missed them both.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5KR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KS">He breasted a gently rising dune (although there was no sand here; the desert was hardpan, and even the harsh winds that blew when dark came raised only an aggravating harsh dust like scouring powder) and saw the kicked remains of a tiny campfire on the lee side, the side the sun would quit earliest. Small signs like this, <a aid="9H5KT"></a>once more affirming the man in black’s possible humanity, never failed to please him. His lips stretched in the pitted, flaked remains of his face. The grin was gruesome, painful. He squatted.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5KU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5KV">His quarry had burned the devil-grass, of course. It was the only thing out here that <span class="italic" aid="9H5L0">would</span> burn. It burned with a greasy, flat light, and it burned slow. Border dwellers had told him that devils lived even in the flames. They burned it but would not look into the light. They said the devils hypnotized, beckoned, would eventually draw the one who looked into the fires. And the next man foolish enough to look into the fire might see you.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5L1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5L2">The burned grass was crisscrossed in the now familiar ideographic pattern, and crumbled to gray senselessness before the gunslinger’s prodding hand. There was nothing in the remains but a charred scrap of bacon, which he ate thoughtfully. It had always been this way. The gunslinger had followed the man in black across the desert for two months now, across the endless, screamingly monotonous purgatorial wastes, and had yet to find spoor other than the hygienic sterile ideographs of the man in black’s campfires. He had not found a can, a bottle, or a waterbag (the gunslinger had left four of those behind, like dead snakeskins). He hadn’t found any dung. He assumed the man in black buried it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5L3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5L4">Perhaps the campfires were a message, spelled out one Great Letter at a time. <span class="italic" aid="9H5L5">Keep your distance, partner,</span> it might say. Or, <span class="italic" aid="9H5L6">The end draweth nigh.</span> Or maybe even, <span class="italic" aid="9H5L7">Come and get me.</span> It didn’t matter what they said or didn’t say. He had no interest in messages, if messages they were. What mattered was that these remains were as cold as all the others. Yet he had gained. He knew he was closer, but did not know how he knew. A kind of smell, perhaps. That didn’t matter, either. He would keep going until something changed, and if nothing changed, he would keep going, anyway. There would be water if God willed it, the oldtimers <a aid="9H5L8"></a>said. Water if God willed it, even in the desert. The gunslinger stood up, brushing his hands.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5L9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LA">No other trace; the wind, razor-sharp, had of course filed away even what scant tracks the hardpan might once have held. No man-scat, no cast-off trash, never a sign of where those things might have been buried. Nothing. Only these cold campfires along the ancient highway moving southeast and the relentless range-finder in his own head. Although of course it was more than that; the pull southeast was more than just a sense of direction, was even more than magnetism.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LC">He sat down and allowed himself a short pull from the waterbag. He thought of that momentary dizziness earlier in the day, that sense of being almost untethered from the world, and wondered what it might have meant. Why should that dizziness make him think of his horn and the last of his old friends, both lost so long ago at Jericho Hill? He still had the guns—his father’s guns—and surely they were more important than horns . . . or even friends.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LE">Weren’t they?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LG">The question was oddly troubling, but since there seemed to be no answer but the obvious one, he put it aside, possibly for later consideration. He scanned the desert and then looked up at the sun, which was now sliding into a far quadrant of the sky that was, disturbingly, not quite true west. He got up, removed his threadbare gloves from his belt, and began to pull devil-grass for his own fire, which he laid over the ashes the man in black had left. He found the irony, like his thirst, bitterly appealing.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LI">He did not take the flint and steel from his purse until the remains of the day were only fugitive heat in the ground beneath him and a sardonic orange line on the monochrome horizon. He sat with his gunna drawn across his lap and watched the southeast <a aid="9H5LJ"></a>patiently, looking toward the mountains, not hoping to see the thin straight line of smoke from a new campfire, not expecting to see an orange spark of flame, but watching anyway because watching was a part of it, and had its own bitter satisfaction. <span class="italic" aid="9H5LK">You will not see what you do not look for, maggot,</span> Cort would have said. <span class="italic" aid="9H5LL">Open the gobs the gods gave ya, will ya not?</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LN">But there was nothing. He was close, but only relatively so. Not close enough to see smoke at dusk, or the orange wink of a campfire.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LP">He laid the flint down the steel rod and struck his spark to the dry, shredded grass, muttering the old and powerful nonsense words as he did: “Spark-a-dark, where’s my sire? Will I lay me? Will I stay me? Bless this camp with fire.” It was strange how some of childhood’s words and ways fell at the wayside and were left behind, while others clamped tight and rode for life, growing the heavier to carry as time passed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LR">He lay down upwind of his little blazon, letting the dream-smoke blow out toward the waste. The wind, except for occasional gyrating dust-devils, was constant.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5LS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5LT">Above, the stars were unwinking, also constant. Suns and worlds by the million. Dizzying constellations, cold fire in every primary hue. As he watched, the sky washed from violet to ebony. A meteor etched a brief, spectacular arc below Old Mother and winked out. The fire threw strange shadows as the devil-grass burned its slow way down into new patterns—not ideograms but a straightforward crisscross vaguely frightening in its own no-nonsense surety. He had laid his fuel in a pattern that was not artful but only workable. It spoke of blacks and whites. It spoke of a man who might straighten bad pictures in strange hotel rooms. The fire burned its steady, slow flame, and phantoms danced in its incandescent core. The gunslinger did not see. The two patterns, <a aid="9H5LU"></a>art and craft, were welded together as he slept. The wind moaned, a witch with cancer in her belly. Every now and then a perverse downdraft would make the smoke whirl and puff toward him and he breathed some of it in. It built dreams in the same way that a small irritant may build a pearl in an oyster. The gunslinger occasionally moaned with the wind. The stars were as indifferent to this as they were to wars, crucifixions, resurrections. This also would have pleased him.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H5LV"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H5M0"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H5M1"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5M2">II</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H5M3"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H5M4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5M5">He had come down off the last of the foothills leading the mule, whose eyes were already dead and bulging with the heat. He had passed the last town three weeks before, and since then there had only been the deserted coach track and an occasional huddle of border dwellers’ sod dwellings. The huddles had degenerated into single dwellings, most inhabited by lepers or madmen. He found the madmen better company. One had given him a stainless steel Silva compass and bade him give it to the Man Jesus. The gunslinger took it gravely. If he saw Him, he would turn over the compass. He did not expect that he would, but anything was possible. Once he saw a taheen—this one a man with a raven’s head—but the misbegotten thing fled at his hail, cawing what might have been words. What might even have been curses.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5M6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5M7">Five days had passed since the last hut, and he had begun to suspect there would be no more when he topped the last eroded hill and saw the familiar low-backed sod roof.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5M8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5M9">The dweller, a surprisingly young man with a wild shock of strawberry hair that reached almost to his waist, was weeding a scrawny stand of corn with zealous abandon. The mule let out a <a aid="9H5MA"></a>wheezing grunt and the dweller looked up, glaring blue eyes coming target-center on the gunslinger in a moment. The dweller was unarmed, with no bolt nor bah the gunslinger could see. He raised both hands in curt salute to the stranger and then bent to the corn again, humping up the row next to his hut with back bent, tossing devil-grass and an occasional stunted corn plant over his shoulder. His hair flopped and flew in the wind that now came directly from the desert, with nothing to break it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MC">The gunslinger came down the hill slowly, leading the donkey on which his waterskins sloshed. He paused by the edge of the lifeless-looking cornpatch, drew a drink from one of his skins to start the saliva, and spat into the arid soil.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5ME">“Life for your crop.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MG">“Life for your own,” the dweller answered and stood up. His back popped audibly. He surveyed the gunslinger without fear. The little of his face visible between beard and hair seemed unmarked by the rot, and his eyes, while a bit wild, seemed sane. “Long days and pleasant nights, stranger.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MI">“And may you have twice the number.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MK">“Unlikely,” the dweller replied, and voiced a curt laugh. “I don’t have nobbut corn and beans,” he said. “Corn’s free, but you’ll have to kick something in for the beans. A man brings them out once in a while. He don’t stay long.” The dweller laughed shortly. “Afraid of spirits. Afraid of the bird-man, too.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5ML"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MM">“I saw him. The bird-man, I mean. He fled me.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MO">“Yar, he’s lost his way. Claims to be looking for a place called Algul Siento, only sometimes he calls it Blue Haven or Heaven, I can’t make out which. Has thee heard of it?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MQ">The gunslinger shook his head.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MS">“Well . . . he don’t bite and he don’t bide, so fuck him. Is thee alive or dead?”</span></p>
<a aid="9H5MT"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5MU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5MV">“Alive,” the gunslinger said. “You speak as the Manni do.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5N0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5N1">“I was with ’em awhile, but that was no life for me; too chummy, they are, and always looking for holes in the world.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5N2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5N3">This was true, the gunslinger reflected. The Manni-folk were great travelers.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5N4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5N5">The two of them looked at each other in silence for a moment, and then the dweller put out his hand. “Brown is my name.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5N6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5N7">The gunslinger shook and gave his own name. As he did so, a scrawny raven croaked from the low peak of the sod roof. The dweller gestured at it briefly: “That’s Zoltan.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5N8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5N9">At the sound of its name the raven croaked again and flew across to Brown. It landed on the dweller’s head and roosted, talons firmly twined in the wild thatch of hair.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NA"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NB">“Screw you,” Zoltan croaked brightly. “Screw you and the horse you rode in on.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5ND">The gunslinger nodded amiably.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NE"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NF">“Beans, beans, the musical fruit,” the raven recited, inspired. “The more you eat, the more you toot.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NG"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NH">“You teach him that?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NJ">“That’s all he wants to learn, I guess,” Brown said. “Tried to teach him The Lord’s Prayer once.” His eyes traveled out beyond the hut for a moment, toward the gritty, featureless hardpan. “Guess this ain’t Lord’s Prayer country. You’re a gunslinger. That right?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NL">“Yes.” He hunkered down and brought out his makings. Zoltan launched himself from Brown’s head and landed, flittering, on the gunslinger’s shoulder.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NN">“Thought your kind was gone.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NP">“Then you see different, don’t you?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NR">“Did’ee come from In-World?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5NT">“Long ago,” the gunslinger agreed.</span></p>
<a aid="9H5NU"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5NV"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5O0">“Anything left there?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5O1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5O2">To this the gunslinger made no reply, but his face suggested this was a topic better not pursued.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5O3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5O4">“After the other one, I guess.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5O5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5O6">“Yes.” The inevitable question followed: “How long since he passed by?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5O7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5O8">Brown shrugged. “I don’t know. Time’s funny out here. Distance and direction, too. More than two weeks. Less than two months. The bean man’s been twice since he passed. I’d guess six weeks. That’s probably wrong.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5O9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OA">“The more you eat, the more you toot,” Zoltan said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OC">“Did he lay by?” the gunslinger asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OE">Brown nodded. “He stayed supper, same as you will, I guess. We passed the time.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OG">The gunslinger stood up and the bird flew back to the roof, squawking. He felt an odd, trembling eagerness. “What did he talk about?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OI">Brown cocked an eyebrow at him. “Not much. Did it ever rain and when did I come here and had I buried my wife. He asked was she of the Manni-folk and I said yar, because it seemed like he already knew. I did most of the talking, which ain’t usual.” He paused, and the only sound was the stark wind. “He’s a sorcerer, ain’t he?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OK">“Among other things.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OM">Brown nodded slowly. “I knew. He dropped a rabbit out of his sleeve, all gutted and ready for the pot. Are you?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5ON"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OO">“A sorcerer?” He laughed. “I’m just a man.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OQ">“You’ll never catch him.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OS">“I’ll catch him.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5OT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5OU">They looked at each other, a sudden depth of feeling between them, the dweller upon his dust-puff-dry ground, the gunslinger <a aid="9H5OV"></a>on the hardpan that shelved down to the desert. He reached for his flint.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5P0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5P1">“Here.” Brown produced a sulfur-headed match and struck it with a grimed nail. The gunslinger pushed the tip of his smoke into the flame and drew.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5P2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5P3">“Thanks.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5P4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5P5">“You’ll want to fill your skins,” the dweller said, turning away. “Spring’s under the eaves in back. I’ll start dinner.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5P6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5P7">The gunslinger stepped gingerly over the rows of corn and went around back. The spring was at the bottom of a hand-dug well, lined with stones to keep the powdery earth from caving. As he descended the rickety ladder, the gunslinger reflected that the stones must represent two years’ work easily—hauling, dragging, laying. The water was clear but slow-moving, and filling the skins was a long chore. While he was topping the second, Zoltan perched on the lip of the well.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5P8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5P9">“Screw you and the horse you rode in on,” he advised.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PA"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PB">The gunslinger looked up, startled. The shaft was about fifteen feet deep: easy enough for Brown to drop a rock on him, break his head, and steal everything on him. A crazy or a rotter wouldn’t do it; Brown was neither. Yet he liked Brown, and so he pushed the thought out of his mind and got the rest of the water God had willed. Whatever else God willed was ka’s business, not his.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PD">When he came through the hut’s door and walked down the steps (the hovel proper was set below ground level, designed to catch and hold the coolness of the nights), Brown was poking ears of corn into the embers of a tiny fire with a crude hardwood spatula. Two ragged plates had been set at opposite ends of a dun blanket. Water for the beans was just beginning to bubble in a pot hung over the fire.</span></p>
<a aid="9H5PE"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PG">“I’ll pay for the water, too.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PI">Brown did not look up. “The water’s a gift from God, as I think thee knows. Pappa Doc brings the beans.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PK">The gunslinger grunted a laugh and sat down with his back against one rude wall, folded his arms, and closed his eyes. After a little, the smell of roasting corn came to his nose. There was a pebbly rattle as Brown dumped a paper of dry beans into the pot. An occasional <span class="italic" aid="9H5PL">tak-tak-tak</span> as Zoltan walked restlessly on the roof. He was tired; he had been going sixteen and sometimes eighteen hours a day between here and the horror that had occurred in Tull, the last village. And he had been afoot for the last twelve days; the mule was at the end of its endurance, only living because it was a habit. Once he had known a boy named Sheemie who’d had a mule. Sheemie was gone now; they were all gone now and there was only the two of them: him, and the man in black. He had heard rumor of other lands beyond this, green lands in a place called Mid-World, but it was hard to believe. Out here, green lands seemed like a child’s fantasy.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PN"><span class="italic" aid="9H5PO">Tak-tak-tak.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PQ">Two weeks, Brown had said, or as many as six. Didn’t matter. There had been calendars in Tull, and they had remembered the man in black because of the old man he had healed on his way through. Just an old man dying of the weed. An old man of thirty-five. And if Brown was right, he had closed a good deal of distance on the man in black since then. But the desert was next. And the desert would be hell.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PS"><span class="italic" aid="9H5PT">Tak-tak-tak . . .</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5PU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5PV"><span class="italic" aid="9H5Q0">Lend me your wings, bird. I’ll spread them and fly on the thermals.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5Q1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5Q2">He slept.</span></p>
<a aid="9H5Q3"></a>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H5Q4"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H5Q5"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H5Q6"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5Q7">III</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H5Q8"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H5Q9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QA">Brown woke him up an hour later. It was dark. The only light was the dull cherry glare of the banked embers.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QC">“Your mule has passed on,” Brown said. “Tell ya sorry. Dinner’s ready.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QE">“How?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QG">Brown shrugged. “Roasted and boiled, how else? You picky?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QI">“No, the mule.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QK">“It just laid over, that’s all. It looked like an old mule.” And with a touch of apology: “Zoltan et the eyes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QM">“Oh.” He might have expected it. “All right.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QO">Brown surprised him again when they sat down to the blanket that served as a table by asking a brief blessing: Rain, health, expansion to the spirit.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QQ">“Do you believe in an afterlife?” the gunslinger asked him as Brown dropped three ears of hot corn onto his plate.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5QR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5QS">Brown nodded. “I think this is it.”</span></p><div class="calibre_5" aid="9H5QT"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H5QU"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H5QV"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5R0">IV</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H5R1"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H5R2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5R3">The beans were like bullets, the corn tough. Outside, the prevailing wind snuffled and whined around the ground-level eaves. The gunslinger ate quickly, ravenously, drinking four cups of water with the meal. Halfway through, there was a machine-gun rapping at the door. Brown got up and let Zoltan in. The bird flew across the room and hunched moodily in the corner.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5R4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5R5">“Musical fruit,” he muttered.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5R6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5R7">“You ever think about eating him?” the gunslinger asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5R8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5R9">The dweller laughed. “Animals that talk be tough,” he said. <a aid="9H5RA"></a>“Birds, billy-bumblers, human beans. They be tough eatin’.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RC">After dinner, the gunslinger offered his tobacco. The dweller, Brown, accepted eagerly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RE"><span class="italic" aid="9H5RF">Now,</span> the gunslinger thought. <span class="italic" aid="9H5RG">Now the questions will come.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RI">But Brown asked no questions. He smoked tobacco that had been grown in Garlan years before and looked at the dying embers of the fire. It was already noticeably cooler in the hovel.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RK">“Lead us not into temptation,” Zoltan said suddenly, apocalyptically.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RM">The gunslinger started as if he had been shot at. He was suddenly sure all this was an illusion, that the man in black had spun a spell and was trying to tell him something in a maddeningly obtuse, symbolic way.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RO">“Do you know Tull?” he asked suddenly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RQ">Brown nodded. “Came through it to get here, went back once to sell corn and drink a glass of whiskey. It rained that year. Lasted maybe fifteen minutes. The ground just seemed to open and suck it up. An hour later it was just as white and dry as ever. But the corn—God, the corn. You could see it grow. That wasn’t so bad. But you could <span class="italic" aid="9H5RR">hear</span> it, as if the rain had given it a mouth. It wasn’t a happy sound. It seemed to be sighing and groaning its way out of the earth.” He paused. “I had extra, so I took it and sold it. Pappa Doc said he’d do it, but he would have cheated me. So I went.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RT">“You don’t like town?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5RU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5RV">“No.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5S0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5S1">“I almost got killed there,” the gunslinger said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5S2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5S3">“Do you say so?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5S4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5S5">“Set my watch and warrant on it. And I killed a man that was touched by God,” the gunslinger said. “Only it wasn’t God. It was the man with the rabbit up his sleeve. The man in black.”</span></p>
<a aid="9H5S6"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5S7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5S8">“He laid you a trap.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5S9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SA">“You say true, I say thank ya.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SC">They looked at each other across the shadows, the moment taking on overtones of finality.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SE">Now <span class="italic" aid="9H5SF">the questions will come.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SG"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SH">But Brown still had no questions to ask. His cigarette was down to a smoldering roach, but when the gunslinger tapped his poke, Brown shook his head.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SJ">Zoltan shifted restlessly, seemed about to speak, subsided.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SL">“Will I tell you about it?” the gunslinger asked. “Ordinarily I’m not much of a talker, but . . .”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SN">“Sometimes talking helps. I’ll listen.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SP">The gunslinger searched for words to begin and found none. “I have to pass water,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SR">Brown nodded. “Pass it in the corn, please.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5ST">“Sure.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5SU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5SV">He went up the stairs and out into the dark. The stars glittered overhead. The wind pulsed. His urine arched out over the powdery cornfield in a wavering stream. The man in black had drawn him here. It wasn’t beyond possibility that Brown <span class="italic" aid="9H5T0">was</span> the man in black. He might be . . .</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5T1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5T2">The gunslinger shut these useless and upsetting thoughts away. The only contingency he had not learned how to bear was the possibility of his own madness. He went back inside.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5T3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5T4">“Have you decided if I’m an enchantment yet?” Brown asked, amused.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5T5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5T6">The gunslinger paused on the tiny landing, startled. Then he came down slowly and sat. “The thought crossed my mind. Are you?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5T7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5T8">“If I am, I don’t know it.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5T9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TA">This wasn’t a terribly helpful answer, but the gunslinger decided to let it pass. “I started to tell you about Tull.”</span></p>
<a aid="9H5TB"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TD">“Is it growing?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TE"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TF">“It’s dead,” the gunslinger said. “I killed it.” He thought of adding: <span class="italic" aid="9H5TG">And now I’m going to kill you, if for no other reason than I don’t want to have to sleep with one eye open.</span> But had he come to such behavior? If so, why bother to go on at all? Why, if he had become what he pursued?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TI">Brown said, “I don’t want nothing from you, gunslinger, except to still be here when you move on. I won’t beg for my life, but that don’t mean I don’t want it yet awhile longer.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TK">The gunslinger closed his eyes. His mind whirled.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TM">“Tell me what you are,” he said thickly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TO">“Just a man. One who means you no harm. And I’m still willing to listen if you’re willing to talk.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TQ">To this the gunslinger made no reply.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TS">“I guess you won’t feel right about it unless I invite you,” Brown said, “and so I do. Will you tell me about Tull?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5TT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5TU">The gunslinger was surprised to find that this time the words were there. He began to speak in flat bursts that slowly spread into an even, slightly toneless narrative. He found himself oddly excited. He talked deep into the night. Brown did not interrupt at all. Neither did the bird.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H5TV"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H5U0"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H5U1"><span class="bold1" aid="9H5U2">V</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H5U3"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H5U4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5U5">He’d bought the mule in Pricetown, and when he reached Tull, it was still fresh. The sun had set an hour earlier, but the gunslinger had continued traveling, guided by the town glow in the sky, then by the uncannily clear notes of a honky-tonk piano playing “Hey Jude.” The road widened as it took on tributaries. Here and there were overhead sparklights, all of them long dead.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5U6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5U7">The forests were long gone now, replaced by the monotonous <a aid="9H5U8"></a>flat prairie country: endless, desolate fields gone to timothy and low shrubs; eerie, deserted estates guarded by brooding, shadowed mansions where demons undeniably walked; leering, empty shanties where the people had either moved on or had been moved along; an occasional dweller’s hovel, given away by a single flickering point of light in the dark, or by sullen, inbred clan-fams toiling silently in the fields by day. Corn was the main crop, but there were beans and also some pokeberries. An occasional scrawny cow stared at him lumpishly from between peeled alder poles. Coaches had passed him four times, twice coming and twice going, nearly empty as they came up on him from behind and bypassed him and his mule, fuller as they headed back toward the forests of the north. Now and then a farmer passed with his feet up on the splashboard of his bucka, careful not to look at the man with the guns.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5U9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UA">It was ugly country. It had showered twice since he had left Pricetown, grudgingly both times. Even the timothy looked yellow and dispirited. Pass-on-by country. He had seen no sign of the man in black. Perhaps he had taken a coach.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UC">The road made a bend, and beyond it the gunslinger clucked the mule to a stop and looked down at Tull. It was at the floor of a circular, bowl-shaped hollow, a shoddy jewel in a cheap setting. There were a number of lights, most of them clustered around the area of the music. There looked to be four streets, three running at right angles to the coach road, which was the main avenue of the town. Perhaps there would be a cafe. He doubted it, but perhaps. He clucked at the mule.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UE">More houses sporadically lined the road now, most of them still deserted. He passed a tiny graveyard with moldy, leaning wooden slabs overgrown and choked by the rank devil-grass. Perhaps five hundred feet further on he passed a chewed sign which said: <span aid="9H5UF"><span class="calibre4" aid="9H5UG">TULL</span></span>.</span></p>
<a aid="9H5UH"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UJ">The paint was flaked almost to the point of illegibility. There was another further on, but the gunslinger was not able to read that one at all.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UL">A fool’s chorus of half-stoned voices was rising in the final protracted lyric of “Hey Jude”—“Naa-naa-naa naa-na-na-na . . . hey, Jude . . .”—as he entered the town proper. It was a dead sound, like the wind in the hollow of a rotted tree. Only the prosaic thump and pound of the honky-tonk piano saved him from seriously wondering if the man in black might not have raised ghosts to inhabit a deserted town. He smiled a little at the thought.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UN">There were people on the streets, but not many. Three ladies wearing black slacks and identical high-collared blouses passed by on the opposite boardwalk, not looking at him with pointed curiosity. Their faces seemed to swim above their all-but-invisible bodies like pallid balls with eyes. A solemn old man with a straw hat perched firmly on top of his head watched him from the steps of a boarded-up mercantile store. A scrawny tailor with a late customer paused to watch him go by; he held up the lamp in his window for a better look. The gunslinger nodded. Neither the tailor nor his customer nodded back. He could feel their eyes resting heavily upon the low-slung holsters that lay against his hips. A young boy, perhaps thirteen, and a girl who might have been his sissa or his jilly-child crossed the street a block up, pausing imperceptibly. Their footfalls raised little hanging clouds of dust. Here in town most of the streetside lamps worked, but they weren’t electric; their isinglass sides were cloudy with congealed oil. Some had been crashed out. There was a livery with a just-hanging-on look to it, probably depending on the coach line for its survival. Three boys were crouched silently around a marble ring drawn in the dust to one side of the barn’s gaping maw, smoking cornshuck cigarettes. They made long shadows in the <a aid="9H5UO"></a>yard. One had a scorpion’s tail poked in the band of his hat. Another had a bloated left eye bulging sightlessly from its socket.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UQ">The gunslinger led his mule past them and looked into the dim depths of the barn. One lamp glowed sunkenly. A shadow jumped and flickered as a gangling old man in bib overalls forked loose timothy hay into the hayloft with big, grunting swipes of his fork.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5US">“Hey!” the gunslinger called.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5UU">The fork faltered and the hostler looked around with yellow-tinged eyes. “Hey yourself!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5UV"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5V0">“I got a mule here.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5V1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5V2">“Good for you.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5V3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5V4">The gunslinger flicked a heavy, unevenly milled gold piece into the semidark. It rang on the old, chaff-drifted boards and glittered.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5V5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5V6">The hostler came forward, bent, picked it up, squinted at the gunslinger. His eyes dropped to the gunbelts and he nodded sourly. “How long you want him put up?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5V7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5V8">“A night or two. Maybe longer.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5V9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VA">“I ain’t got no change for gold.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VC">“Didn’t ask for any.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VE">“Shoot-up money,” the hostler muttered.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VG">“What did you say?”</span></p>
