<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VI">“Nothing.” The hostler caught the mule’s bridle and led him inside.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VK">“Rub him down!” the gunslinger called. “I expect to smell it on him when I come back, hear me well!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VM">The old man did not turn. The gunslinger walked out to the boys crouched around the marble ring. They had watched the entire exchange with contemptuous interest.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VO">“Long days and pleasant nights,” the gunslinger offered conversationally.</span></p>
<a aid="9H5VP"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VR">No answer.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VT">“You fellas live in town?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H5VU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H5VV">No answer, unless the scorpion’s tail gave one: it seemed to nod.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H600"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H601">One of the boys removed a crazily tilted twist of cornshuck from his mouth, grasped a green cat’s-eye marble, and squirted it into the dirt circle. It struck a croaker and knocked it outside. He picked up the cat’s-eye and prepared to shoot again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H602"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H603">“There a cafe in this town?” the gunslinger asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H604"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H605">One of them looked up, the youngest. There was a huge cold-sore at the corner of his mouth, but his eyes were both the same size, and full of an innocence that wouldn’t last long in this shithole. He looked at the gunslinger with hooded brimming wonder that was touching and frightening.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H606"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H607">“Might get a burger at Sheb’s.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H608"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H609">“That the honky-tonk?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60B">The boy nodded. “Yar.” The eyes of his mates had turned ugly and hostile. He would probably pay for having spoken up in kindness.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60C"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60D">The gunslinger touched the brim of his hat. “I’m grateful. It’s good to know someone in this town is bright enough to talk.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60E"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60F">He walked past, mounted the boardwalk, and started down toward Sheb’s, hearing the clear, contemptuous voice of one of the others, hardly more than a childish treble: “Weed-eater! How long you been screwin’ your sister, Charlie? Weed-eater!” Then the sound of a blow and a cry.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60G"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60H">There were three flaring kerosene lamps in front of Sheb’s, one to each side and one nailed above the drunk-hung batwing doors. The chorus of “Hey Jude” had petered out, and the piano was plinking some other old ballad. Voices murmured like broken threads. The gunslinger paused outside for a moment, looking in. Sawdust floor, spittoons by the tipsy-legged tables. A plank bar <a aid="9H60I"></a>on sawhorses. A gummy mirror behind it, reflecting the piano player, who wore an inevitable piano-stool slouch. The front of the piano had been removed so you could watch the wooden keys whonk up and down as the contraption was played. The bartender was a straw-haired woman wearing a dirty blue dress. One strap was held with a safety pin. There were perhaps six townies in the back of the room, juicing and playing Watch Me apathetically. Another half-dozen were grouped loosely about the piano. Four or five at the bar. And an old man with wild gray hair collapsed at a table by the doors. The gunslinger went in.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60K">Heads swiveled to look at him and his guns. There was a moment of near silence, except for the oblivious piano player, who continued to tinkle. Then the woman mopped at the bar, and things shifted back.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60L"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60M">“Watch me,” one of the players in the corner said and matched three hearts with four spades, emptying his hand. The one with the hearts swore, pushed over his stake, and the next hand was dealt.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60N"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60O">The gunslinger approached the woman at the bar. “You got meat?” he asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60P"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60Q">“Sure.” She looked him in the eye, and she might have been pretty when she started out, but the world had moved on since then. Now her face was lumpy and there a livid scar went corkscrewing across her forehead. She had powdered it heavily, and the powder called attention to what it had been meant to camouflage. “Clean beef. Threaded stock. It’s dear, though.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60R"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H60S"><span class="italic" aid="9H60T">Threaded stock, my ass,</span> the gunslinger thought. <span class="italic" aid="9H60U">What you got in your cooler came from something with three eyes, six legs, or both—that’s my guess, lady-sai.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H60V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H610">“I want three burgers and a beer, would it please ya.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H611"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H612">Again that subtle shift in tone. Three hamburgers. Mouths <a aid="9H613"></a>watered and tongues licked at saliva with slow lust. Three hamburgers. Had anyone here ever seen anyone eat three hamburgers at a go?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H614"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H615">“That would go you five bocks. Do you ken bocks?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H616"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H617">“Dollars?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H618"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H619">She nodded, so she was probably saying <span class="italic" aid="9H61A">bucks.</span> That was his guess, anyway.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61B"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61C">“That with the beer?” he asked, smiling a little. “Or is the beer extra?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61D"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61E">She didn’t return the smile. “I’ll throw in the suds. Once I see the color of your money, that is.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61F"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61G">The gunslinger put a gold piece on the bar, and every eye followed it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61H"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61I">There was a smoldering charcoal cooker behind the bar and to the left of the mirror. The woman disappeared into a small room behind it and returned with meat on a paper. She scrimped out three patties and put them on the grill. The smell that arose was maddening. The gunslinger stood with stolid indifference, only peripherally aware of the faltering piano, the slowing of the card game, the sidelong glances of the barflies.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61K">The man was halfway up behind him when the gunslinger saw him in the mirror. The man was almost completely bald, and his hand was wrapped around the haft of a gigantic hunting knife that was looped onto his belt like a holster.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61L"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61M">“Go sit down,” the gunslinger said. “Do yourself a favor, cully.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61N"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61O">The man stopped. His upper lip lifted unconsciously, like a dog’s, and there was a moment of silence. Then he went back to his table, and the atmosphere shifted back again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61P"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61Q">Beer came in a cracked glass schooner. “I ain’t got change for gold,” the woman said truculently.</span></p>
<a aid="9H61R"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61S"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61T">“Don’t expect any.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H61U"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H61V">She nodded angrily, as if this show of wealth, even at her benefit, incensed her. But she took his gold, and a moment later the hamburgers came on a cloudy plate, still red around the edges.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H620"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H621">“Do you have salt?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H622"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H623">She gave it to him in a little crock she took from underneath the bar, white lumps he’d have to crumble with his fingers. “Bread?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H624"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H625">“No bread.” He knew she was lying, but he also knew why and didn’t push it. The bald man was staring at him with cya-nosed eyes, his hands clenching and unclenching on the splintered and gouged surface of his table. His nostrils flared with pulsating regularity, scooping up the smell of the meat. That, at least, was free.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H626"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H627">The gunslinger began to eat steadily, not seeming to taste, merely chopping the meat apart and forking it into his mouth, trying not to think of what the cow this had come from must have looked like. Threaded stock, she had said. Yes, quite likely! And pigs would dance the commala in the light of the Peddler’s Moon.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H628"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H629">He was almost through, ready to call for another beer and roll a smoke, when the hand fell on his shoulder.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62B">He suddenly became aware that the room had once more gone silent, and he tasted tension in the air. He turned around and stared into the face of the man who had been asleep by the door when he entered. It was a terrible face. The odor of the devil-grass was a rank miasma. The eyes were damned, the staring, glaring eyes of one who sees but does not see, eyes ever turned inward to the sterile hell of dreams beyond control, dreams unleashed, risen out of the stinking swamps of the unconscious.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62C"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62D">The woman behind the bar made a small moaning sound.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62E"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62F">The cracked lips writhed, lifted, revealing the green, mossy <a aid="9H62G"></a>teeth, and the gunslinger thought: <span class="italic" aid="9H62H">He’s not even smoking it anymore. He’s chewing it. He’s really</span> chewing <span class="italic" aid="9H62I">it.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62K">And on the heels of that: <span class="italic" aid="9H62L">He’s a dead man. He should have been dead a year ago.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62M"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62N">And on the heels of that: <span class="italic" aid="9H62O">The man in black did this.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62P"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62Q">They stared at each other, the gunslinger and the man who had gone around the rim of madness.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62R"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62S">He spoke, and the gunslinger, dumbfounded, heard himself addressed in the High Speech of Gilead.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62T"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H62U">“The gold for a favor, gunslinger-sai. Just one? For a pretty.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H62V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H630">The High Speech. For a moment his mind refused to track it. It had been years—God!—centuries, millenniums; there was no more High Speech; he was the last, the last gunslinger. The others were all . . .</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H631"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H632">Numbed, he reached into his breast pocket and produced a gold piece. The split, scabbed, gangrenous hand reached for it, fondled it, held it up to reflect the greasy glare of the kerosene lamps. It threw off its proud civilized glow; golden, reddish, bloody.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H633"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H634"><span class="italic" aid="9H635">“Ahhhhhh . . .”</span> An inarticulate sound of pleasure. The old man did a weaving turn and began moving back to his table, holding the coin at eye level, turning it, flashing it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H636"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H637">The room was emptying rapidly, the batwings shuttling madly back and forth. The piano player closed the lid of his instrument with a bang and exited after the others in long, comic-opera strides.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H638"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H639">“Sheb!” the woman screamed after him, her voice an odd mixture of fear and shrewishness, “Sheb, you come back here! Goddammit!” Was that a name the gunslinger had heard before? He thought yes, but there was no time to reflect upon it now, or to cast his mind back.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63B">The old man, meanwhile, had gone back to his table. He spun <a aid="9H63C"></a>the gold piece on the gouged wood, and the dead-alive eyes followed it with empty fascination. He spun it a second time, a third, and his eyelids drooped. The fourth time, and his head settled to the wood before the coin stopped.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63D"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63E">“There,” she said softly, furiously. “You’ve driven out my trade. Are you satisfied?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63F"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63G">“They’ll be back,” the gunslinger said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63H"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63I">“Not tonight they won’t.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63K">“Who is he?” He gestured at the weed-eater.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63L"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63M">“Go fuck yourself. <span class="italic" aid="9H63N">Sai.</span>”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63O"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63P">“I have to know,” the gunslinger said patiently. “He—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63Q"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63R">“He talked to you funny,” she said. “Nort never talked like that in his life.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63S"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63T">“I’m looking for a man. You would know him.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H63U"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H63V">She stared at him, the anger dying. It was replaced with speculation, then with a high, wet gleam he had seen before. The rickety building ticked thoughtfully to itself. A dog barked brayingly, far away. The gunslinger waited. She saw his knowledge and the gleam was replaced by hopelessness, by a dumb need that had no mouth.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H640"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H641">“I guess maybe you know my price,” she said. “I got an itch I used to be able to take care of, but now I can’t.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H642"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H643">He looked at her steadily. The scar would not show in the dark. Her body was lean enough so the desert and grit and grind hadn’t been able to sag everything. And she’d once been pretty, maybe even beautiful. Not that it mattered. It would not have mattered if the grave-beetles had nested in the arid blackness of her womb. It had all been written. Somewhere some hand had put it all down in ka’s book.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H644"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H645">Her hands came up to her face and there was still some juice left in her—enough to weep.</span></p>
<a aid="9H646"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H647"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H648">“Don’t <span class="italic" aid="9H649">look!</span> You don’t have to look at me so mean!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64B">“I’m sorry,” the gunslinger said. “I didn’t mean to be mean.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64C"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64D">“None of you mean it!” she cried at him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64E"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64F">“Close the place up and put out the lights.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64G"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64H">She wept, hands at her face. He was glad she had her hands at her face. Not because of the scar but because it gave her back her maidenhood, if not her maidenhead. The pin that held the strap of her dress glittered in the greasy light.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64I"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64J">“Will he steal anything? I’ll put him out if he will.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64K"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64L">“No,” she whispered. “Nort don’t steal.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64M"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64N">“Then put out the lights.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H64O"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H64P">She would not remove her hands until she was behind him and she doused the lamps one by one, turning down the wicks and breathing the flames into extinction. Then she took his hand in the dark and it was warm. She led him upstairs. There was no light to hide their act.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H64Q"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H64R"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H64S"><span class="bold1" aid="9H64T">VI</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H64U"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H64V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H650">He made cigarettes in the dark, then lit them and passed one to her. The room held her scent, fresh lilac, pathetic. The smell of the desert had overlaid it. He realized he was afraid of the desert ahead.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H651"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H652">“His name is Nort,” she said. No harshness had been worn out of her voice. “Just Nort. He died.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H653"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H654">The gunslinger waited.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H655"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H656">“He was touched by God.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H657"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H658">The gunslinger said, “I have never seen Him.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H659"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65A">“He was here ever since I can remember—Nort, I mean, not God.” She laughed jaggedly into the dark. “He had a <a aid="9H65B"></a>honey-wagon for a while. Started to drink. Started to smell the grass. Then to smoke it. The kids started to follow him around and sic their dogs onto him. He wore old green pants that stank. Do you understand?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65C"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65D">“Yes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65E"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65F">“He started to chew it. At the last he just sat in there and didn’t eat anything. He might have been a king, in his mind. The children might have been his jesters, and the dogs his princes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65G"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65H">“Yes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65I"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65J">“He died right in front of this place,” she said. “Came clumping down the boardwalk—his boots wouldn’t wear out, they were engineer boots he found in the old trainyard—with the children and dogs behind him. He looked like wire clothes hangers all wrapped and twirled together. You could see all the lights of hell in his eyes, but he was grinning, just like the grins the children carve into their sharproots and pumpkins, come Reap. You could smell the dirt and the rot and the weed. It was running down from the corners of his mouth like green blood. I think he meant to come in and listen to Sheb play the piano. And right in front, he stopped and cocked his head. I could see him, and I thought he heard a coach, although there was none due. Then he puked, and it was black and full of blood. It went right through that grin like sewer water through a grate. The stink was enough to make you want to run mad. He raised up his arms and just threw over. That was all. He died in his own vomit with that grin on his face.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65K"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65L">“A nice story.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65M"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65N">“Oh yes, thankee-sai. This be a nice place.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65O"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65P">She was trembling beside him. Outside, the wind kept up its steady whine, and somewhere far away a door was banging, like a sound heard in a dream. Mice ran in the walls. The gunslinger thought in the back of his mind that it was probably the only <a aid="9H65Q"></a>place in town prosperous enough to support mice. He put a hand on her belly and she started violently, then relaxed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65R"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65S">“The man in black,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65T"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H65U">“You have to have it, don’t you? You couldn’t just throw me a fuck and go to sleep.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H65V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H660">“I have to have it.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H661"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H662">“All right. I’ll tell you.” She grasped his hand in both of hers and told him.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H663"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H664"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H665"><span class="bold1" aid="9H666">VII</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H667"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H668"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H669">He came in the late afternoon of the day Nort died, and the wind was whooping it up, pulling away the loose topsoil, sending sheets of grit and uprooted stalks of corn windmilling past. Jubal Kennerly had padlocked the livery, and the few other merchants had shuttered their windows and laid boards across the shutters. The sky was the yellow color of old cheese and the clouds flew across it, as if they had seen something horrifying in the desert wastes where they had so lately been.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66B">The gunslinger’s quarry came in a rickety rig with a rippling tarp tied across its bed. There was a big howdy-do of a grin on his face. They watched him come, and old man Kennerly, lying by the window with a bottle in one hand and the loose, hot flesh of his second-eldest daughter’s left breast in the other, resolved not to be there if he should knock.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66C"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66D">But the man in black went by without slowing the bay that pulled his rig, and the spinning wheels spumed up dust that the wind clutched eagerly. He might have been a priest or a monk; he wore a black robe that had been floured with dust, and a loose hood covered his head and obscured his features, but not that <a aid="9H66E"></a>horrid happy grin. The robe rippled and flapped. From beneath the garment’s hem there peeped heavy buckled boots with square toes.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66F"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66G">He pulled up in front of Sheb’s and tethered the horse, which lowered its head and grunted at the ground. Around the back of the rig he untied one flap, found a weathered saddlebag, threw it over his shoulder, and went in through the batwings.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66H"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66I">Alice watched him curiously, but no one else noticed his arrival. The regulars were drunk as lords. Sheb was playing Methodist hymns ragtime, and the grizzled layabouts who had come in early to avoid the storm and to attend Nort’s wake had sung themselves hoarse. Sheb, drunk nearly to the point of senselessness, intoxicated and horny with his own continued existence, played with hectic, shuttlecock speed, fingers flying like looms.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66K">Voices screeched and hollered, never overcoming the wind but sometimes seeming to challenge it. In the corner, Zachary had thrown Amy Feldon’s skirts over her head and was painting Reap-charms on her knees. A few other women circulated. A fever seemed to be on all of them. The dull stormglow that filtered through the batwings seemed to mock them, however.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66L"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66M">Nort had been laid out on two tables in the center of the room. His engineer boots made a mystical V. His mouth hung open in a slack grin, although someone had closed his eyes and put slugs on them. His hands had been folded on his chest with a sprig of devil-grass in them. He smelled like poison.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66N"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66O">The man in black pushed back his hood and came to the bar. Alice watched him, feeling trepidation mixed with the familiar want that hid within her. There was no religious symbol on him, although that meant nothing by itself.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66P"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66Q">“Whiskey,” he said. His voice was soft and pleasant. “I want the good stuff, honey.”</span></p>
<a aid="9H66R"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66S"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66T">She reached under the counter and brought out a bottle of Star. She could have palmed off the local popskull on him as her best, but did not. She poured, and the man in black watched her. His eyes were large, luminous. The shadows were too thick to determine their color exactly. Her need intensified. The hollering and whooping went on behind, unabated. Sheb, the worthless gelding, was playing about the Christian Soldiers and somebody had persuaded Aunt Mill to sing. Her voice, warped and distorted, cut through the babble like a dull ax through a calf’s brain.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H66U"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H66V">“Hey, Allie!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H670"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H671">She went to serve, resentful of the stranger’s silence, resentful of his no-color eyes and her own restless groin. She was afraid of her needs. They were capricious and beyond her control. They might be the signal of change, which would in turn signal the beginning of her old age—a condition which in Tull was usually as short and bitter as a winter sunset.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H672"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H673">She drew beer until the keg was empty, then broached another. She knew better than to ask Sheb; he would come willingly enough, like the dog he was, and would either chop off his own fingers or spume beer all over everything. The stranger’s eyes were on her as she went about it; she could feel them.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H674"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H675">“It’s busy,” he said when she returned. He had not touched his drink, merely rolled it between his palms to warm it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H676"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H677">“Wake,” she said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H678"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H679">“I noticed the departed.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67B">“They’re bums,” she said with sudden hatred. “All bums.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67C"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67D">“It excites them. He’s dead. They’re not.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67E"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67F">“He was their butt when he was alive. It’s not right that he should be their butt now. It’s . . .” She trailed off, not able to express what it was, or how it was obscene.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67G"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67H">“Weed-eater?”</span></p>
<a aid="9H67I"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67K">“Yes! What else did he have?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67L"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67M">Her tone was accusing, but he did not drop his eyes, and she felt the blood rush to her face. “I’m sorry. Are you a priest? This must revolt you.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67N"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67O">“I’m not and it doesn’t.” He knocked the whiskey back neatly and did not grimace. “Once more, please. Once more with feeling, as they say in the world next door.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67P"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67Q">She had no idea what that might mean, and was afraid to ask. “I’ll have to see the color of your coin first. I’m sorry.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67R"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67S">“No need to be.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67T"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H67U">He put a rough silver coin on the counter, thick on one edge, thin on the other, and she said as she would say later: “I don’t have change for this.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H67V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H680">He shook his head, dismissing it, and watched absently as she poured again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H681"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H682">“Are you only passing through?” she asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H683"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H684">He did not reply for a long time, and she was about to repeat when he shook his head impatiently. “Don’t talk trivialities. You’re here with death.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H685"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H686">She recoiled, hurt and amazed, her first thought being that he had lied about his holiness to test her.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H687"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H688">“You cared for him,” he said flatly. “Isn’t that true?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H689"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68A">“Who? Nort?” She laughed, affecting annoyance to cover her confusion. “I think you better—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68B"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68C">“You’re soft-hearted and a little afraid,” he went on, “and he was on the weed, looking out hell’s back door. And there he is, they’ve even slammed the door now, and you don’t think they’ll open it until it’s time for you to walk through, isn’t it so?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68D"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68E">“What are you, drunk?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68F"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68G">“Mistuh Norton, he <span class="italic" aid="9H68H">daid,</span>” the man in black intoned, giving the words a sardonic little twist. “Dead as anybody. Dead as you or anybody.”</span></p>
<a aid="9H68I"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68J"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68K">“Get out of my place.” She felt a trembling loathing spring up in her, but the warmth still radiated from her belly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68L"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68M">“It’s all right,” he said softly. “It’s all right. Wait. Just wait.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68N"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68O">The eyes were blue. She felt suddenly easy in her mind, as if she had taken a drug.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68P"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68Q">“Dead as anybody,” he said. “Do you see?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68R"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68S">She nodded dumbly and he laughed aloud—a fine, strong, untainted laugh that swung heads around. He whirled and faced them, suddenly the center of attention. Aunt Mill faltered and subsided, leaving a cracked high note bleeding on the air. Sheb struck a discord and halted. They looked at the stranger uneasily. Sand rattled against the sides of the building.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68T"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H68U">The silence held, spun itself out. Her breath had clogged in her throat and she looked down and saw both hands pressed to her belly beneath the bar. They all looked at him and he looked at them. Then the laugh burst forth again, strong, rich, beyond denial. But there was no urge to laugh along with him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H68V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H690">“I’ll show you a wonder!” he cried at them. But they only watched him, like obedient children taken to see a magician in whom they have grown too old to believe.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H691"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H692">The man in black sprang forward, and Aunt Mill drew away from him. He grinned fiercely and slapped her broad belly. A short, unwitting cackle was forced out of her, and the man in black threw back his head.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H693"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H694">“It’s better, isn’t it?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H695"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H696">Aunt Mill cackled again, suddenly broke into sobs, and fled blindly through the doors. The others watched her go silently. The storm was beginning; shadows followed each other, rising and falling on the white cyclorama of the sky. A man near the piano with a forgotten beer in one hand made a groaning, slobbering sound.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H697"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H698">The man in black stood over Nort, grinning down at him. The <a aid="9H699"></a>wind howled and shrieked and thrummed. Something large struck the side of the building hard enough to make it shake and then bounced away. One of the men at the bar tore himself free and headed for some quieter locale, moving in great grotesque strides. Thunder racketed the sky with a sound like some god coughing.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69A"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69B">“All <span class="italic" aid="9H69C">right!</span>” the man in black grinned. “All right, let’s get down <span class="italic" aid="9H69D">to</span> it!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69E"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69F">He began to spit into Nort’s face, aiming carefully. The spittle gleamed on the corpse’s forehead, pearled down the shaven beak of his nose.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69G"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69H">Under the bar, her hands worked faster.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69I"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69J">Sheb laughed, loon-like, and hunched over. He began to cough up phlegm, huge and sticky gobs of it, and let fly. The man in black roared approval and pounded him on the back. Sheb grinned, one gold tooth twinkling.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69K"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69L">Some fled. Others gathered in a loose ring around Nort. His face and the dewlapped rooster-wrinkles of his neck and upper chest gleamed with liquid—liquid so precious in this dry country. And suddenly the rain of spit stopped, as if on signal. There was ragged, heavy breathing.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69M"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69N">The man in black suddenly lunged across the body, jackknifing over it in a smooth arc. It was pretty, like a flash of water. He caught himself on his hands, sprang to his feet in a twist, grinning, and went over again. One of the watchers forgot himself, began to applaud, and suddenly backed away, eyes cloudy with terror. He slobbered a hand across his mouth and made for the door.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69O"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69P">Nort twitched the third time the man in black went across.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69Q"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69R">A sound went through the watchers—a grunt—and then they were silent. The man in black threw his head back and howled. His chest moved in a quick, shallow rhythm as he sucked air. He began to go back and forth at a faster clip, pouring over Nort’s <a aid="9H69S"></a>body like water poured from one glass to another and then back again. The only sound in the room was the tearing rasp of his respiration and the rising pulse of the storm.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69T"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H69U">There came the moment when Nort drew a deep, dry breath. His hands rattled and pounded aimlessly on the table. Sheb screeched and exited. One of the women followed him, her eyes wide and her wimple billowing.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H69V"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6A0">The man in black went across once more, twice, thrice. The body on the table was vibrating now, trembling and rapping and twitching like a large yet essentially lifeless doll with some monstrous clockwork hidden inside. The smell of rot and excrement and decay billowed up in choking waves. There came a moment when his eyes opened.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6A1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6A2">Allie felt her numb and feelingless feet propelling her backward. She struck the mirror, making it shiver, and blind panic took over. She bolted like a steer.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6A3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6A4">“So here’s your wonder,” the man in black called after her, panting. “I’ve given it to you. Now you can sleep easy. Even <span class="italic" aid="9H6A5">that</span> isn’t irreversible. Although it’s . . . so . . . goddamned . . . <span class="italic" aid="9H6A6">funny!</span>” And he began to laugh again. The sound faded as she raced up the stairs, not stopping until the door to the three rooms above the bar was bolted.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6A7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6A8">She began to giggle then, rocking back and forth on her haunches by the door. The sound rose to a keening wail that mixed with the wind. She kept hearing the sound Nort had made when he came back to life—the sound of fists knocking blindly on the lid of a coffin. What thoughts, she wondered, could be left in his reanimated brain? What had he seen while dead? How much did he remember? Would he tell? Were the secrets of the grave waiting downstairs? The most terrible thing about such questions, she reckoned, was that part of you really wanted to ask.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6A9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AA">Below her, Nort wandered absently out into the storm to pull <a aid="9H6AB"></a>some weed. The man in black, now the only patron in the bar, perhaps watched him go, perhaps still grinning.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AD">When she forced herself to go back down that evening, carrying a lamp in one hand and a heavy stick of stovewood in the other, the man in black was gone, rig and all. But Nort was there, sitting at the table by the door as if he had never been away. The smell of the weed was on him, but not as heavily as she might have expected.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AE"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AF">He looked up at her and smiled tentatively. “Hello, Allie.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AG"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AH">“Hello, Nort.” She put the stovewood down and began lighting the lamps, not turning her back to him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AJ">“I been touched by God,” he said presently. “I ain’t going to die no more. He said so. It was a promise.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AL">“How nice for you, Nort.” The spill she was holding dropped through her trembling fingers and she picked it up.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AN">“I’d like to stop chewing the grass,” he said. “I don’t enjoy it no more. It don’t seem right for a man touched by God to be chewing the weed.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AP">“Then why don’t you stop?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AR">Her exasperation had startled her into looking at him as a man again, rather than an infernal miracle. What she saw was a rather sad-looking specimen only half-stoned, looking hangdog and ashamed. She could not be frightened by him anymore.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6AT">“I shake,” he said. “And I want it. I can’t stop. Allie, you was always good to me . . .” He began to weep. “I can’t even stop peeing myself. What am I? <span class="italic" aid="9H6AU">What am I?</span>”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6AV"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6B0">She walked to the table and hesitated there, uncertain.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6B1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6B2">“He could have made me not want it,” he said through the tears. “He could have done that if he could have made me be alive. I ain’t complaining . . . I don’t want to complain . . .” He stared around hauntedly and whispered, “He might strike me dead if I did.”</span></p>
<a aid="9H6B3"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6B4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6B5">“Maybe it’s a joke. He seemed to have quite a sense of humor.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6B6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6B7">Nort took his poke from where it dangled inside his shirt and brought out a handful of grass. Unthinkingly she knocked it away and then drew her hand back, horrified.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6B8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6B9">“I can’t help it, Allie, I can’t,” and he made a crippled dive for the poke. She could have stopped him, but she made no effort. She went back to lighting the lamps, tired although the evening had barely begun. But nobody came in that night except old man Kennerly, who had missed everything. He did not seem particularly surprised to see Nort. Perhaps someone had told him what had happened. He ordered beer, asked where Sheb was, and pawed her.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BA"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6BB">Later, Nort came to her and held out a folded piece of paper in one shaky no-right-to-be-alive hand. “He left you this,” he said. “I near forgot. If I’d forgot, he woulda come back and killed me, sure.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6BD">Paper was valuable, a commodity much to be treasured, but she didn’t like to handle this. It felt heavy, nasty. Written on it was a single word:</span></p>
<p class="calibre10" aid="9H6BE"> </p>
<p class="calibre_" aid="9H6BF"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6BG">Allie</span></p>
<p class="calibre10" aid="9H6BH"> </p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6BJ">“How’d he know my name?” she asked Nort, and Nort only shook his head.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6BL">She opened it and read this:</span></p>
<blockquote class="calibre17" aid="9H6BM"><p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6BN"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6BO">You want to know about Death. I left him a word. That word is NINETEEN. If you say it to him his mind will be opened. He will tell you what lies beyond. He will tell you what he saw.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BP"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6BQ">The word is NINETEEN.</span></p>
<a aid="9H6BR"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BS"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6BT">Knowing will drive you mad. </span></p><p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6BU"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6BV">But sooner or later you will ask. </span></p><p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6C0"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6C1">You won’t be able to help yourself. </span></p><p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6C2"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6C3">Have a nice day! <img alt="smiley.jpg" class="calibre13" src="kindle:embed:000I?mime=image/jpeg"/></span></p>
<p class="calibre_" aid="9H6C4"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6C5">Walter o’ Dim</span></p><p class="calibre10" aid="9H6C6"> </p>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6C7"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6C8">P.S. The word is NINETEEN.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6C9"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6CA">You will try to forget but sooner or later it will come out of your mouth like vomit. </span></p><p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CB"><span class="calibre16" aid="9H6CC">NINETEEN.</span></p></blockquote>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6CE">And oh dear God, she knew that she would. Already it trembled on her lips. <span class="italic" aid="9H6CF">Nineteen,</span> she would say—<span class="italic" aid="9H6CG">Nort, listen: Nineteen.</span> And the secrets of Death and the land beyond would be opened to her.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6CI"><span class="italic" aid="9H6CJ">Sooner or later you will ask.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6CL">The next day things were almost normal, although none of the children followed Nort. The day after that, the catcalls resumed. Life had gotten back on its own sweet keel. The uprooted corn was gathered together by the children, and a week after Nort’s resurrection, they burned it in the middle of the street. The fire was momentarily bright and most of the barflies stepped or staggered out to watch. They looked primitive. Their faces seemed to float between the flames and the ice-chip brilliance of the sky. Allie watched them and felt a pang of fleeting despair for the sad times of this world. The loss. Things had stretched apart. There was no glue at the center anymore. Somewhere something was tottering, and when it fell, all would end. She had never seen the ocean, never would.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6CN">“If I had <span class="italic" aid="9H6CO">guts,</span>” she murmured. “If I had guts, guts, <span class="italic" aid="9H6CP">guts</span> . . .”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6CR">Nort raised his head at the sound of her voice and smiled <a aid="9H6CS"></a>emptily at her from hell. She had no guts. Only a bar and a scar. And a word. It struggled behind her closed lips. Suppose she were to call him over now and draw him close despite his stink? Suppose she said the word into the waxy buggerlug he called an ear? His eyes would change. They would turn into <span class="italic" aid="9H6CT">his</span> eyes—those of the man in the robe. And then Nort would tell what he’d seen in the Land of Death, what lay beyond the earth and the worms.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6CU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6CV"><span class="italic" aid="9H6D0">I’ll never say that word to him.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6D1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6D2">But the man who had brought Nort back to life and left her a note—left her a word like a cocked pistol she would someday put to her temple—had known better.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6D3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6D4">Nineteen would open the secret.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6D5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6D6">Nineteen <span class="italic" aid="9H6D7">was</span> the secret.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6D8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6D9">She caught herself writing it in a puddle on the bar—19—and skidded it to nothingness when she saw Nort watching her.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DA"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DB">The fire burned down rapidly and her customers came back in. She began to dose herself with the Star Whiskey, and by midnight she was blackly drunk.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H6DC"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H6DD"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H6DE"><span class="bold1" aid="9H6DF">VIII</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H6DG"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6DH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DI">She ceased her narrative, and when he made no immediate comment, she thought at first that the story had put him to sleep. She began to drowse herself when he asked: “That’s all?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DK">“Yes. That’s all. It’s very late.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DM">“Um.” He was rolling another cigarette.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DO">“Don’t go getting your tobacco dandruff in my bed,” she told him, more sharply than she had intended.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DQ">“No.”</span></p>
<a aid="9H6DR"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DT">Silence again. The tip of his cigarette winked off and on.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6DU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6DV">“You’ll be leaving in the morning,” she said dully.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6E0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6E1">“I should. I think he’s left a trap for me here. Just like he left one for you.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6E2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6E3">“Do you really think that number would—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6E4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6E5">“If you like your sanity, you don’t ever want to say that word to Nort,” the gunslinger said. “Put it out of your head. If you can, teach yourself that the number after eighteen is twenty. That half of thirty-eight is seventeen. The man who signed himself Walter o’ Dim is a lot of things, but a liar isn’t one of them.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6E6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6E7">“But—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6E8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6E9">“When the urge comes and it’s strong, come up here and hide under your quilts and say it over and over again—scream it, if you have to—until the urge passes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EA"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6EB">“A time will come when it won’t pass.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6ED">The gunslinger made no reply, for he knew this was true. The trap had a ghastly perfection. If someone told you you’d go to hell if you thought about seeing your mother naked (once when the gunslinger was very young he had been told this very thing), you’d eventually do it. And why? Because you did <span class="italic" aid="9H6EE">not</span> want to imagine your mother naked. Because you did <span class="italic" aid="9H6EF">not</span> want to go to hell. Because, if given a knife and a hand in which to hold it, the mind would eventually eat itself. Not because it wanted to; because it did <span class="italic" aid="9H6EG">not</span> want to.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6EI">Sooner or later Allie would call Nort over and say the word.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6EK">“Don’t go,” she said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6EM">“We’ll see.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6EO">He turned on his side away from her, but she was comforted. He would stay, at least for a little while. She drowsed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6EP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6EQ">On the edge of sleep she thought again about the way Nort had addressed him, in that strange talk. It was the only time she <a aid="9H6ER"></a>had seen her strange new lover express emotion. Even his love-making had been a silent thing, and only at the last had his breathing roughened and then stopped for a second or two. He was like something out of a fairytale or a myth, a fabulous, dangerous creature. Could he grant wishes? She thought the answer was yes, and that she would have hers. He would stay awhile. That was wish enough for a luckless scarred bitch such as she. Tomorrow was time enough to think of another, or a third. She slept.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H6ES"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H6ET"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H6EU"><span class="bold1" aid="9H6EV">IX</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H6F0"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6F1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6F2">In the morning she cooked him grits, which he ate without comment. He shoveled them in without thinking about her, hardly seeing her. He knew he should go. Every minute he sat here the man in black was further away—probably out of the hardpan and arroyos and into the desert by now. His path had been undeviatingly southeast, and the gunslinger knew why.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6F3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6F4">“Do you have a map?” he asked, looking up.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6F5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6F6">“Of the town?” she laughed. “There isn’t enough of it to need a map.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6F7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6F8">“No. Of what’s southeast of here.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6F9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FA">Her smile faded. “The desert. Just the desert. I thought you’d stay for a little.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FC">“What’s on the other side of the desert?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FE">“How would I know? Nobody crosses it. Nobody’s tried since I was here.” She wiped her hands on her apron, got potholders, and dumped the tub of water she had been heating into the sink, where it splashed and steamed. “The clouds all go that way. It’s like something sucks them—”</span></p>
<a aid="9H6FF"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FG"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FH">He got up.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FJ">“Where are you going?” She heard the shrill fear in her voice and hated it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FL">“To the stable. If anyone knows, the hostler will.” He put his hands on her shoulders. The hands were hard, but they were also warm. “And to arrange for my mule. If I’m going to be here, he should be taken care of. For when I leave.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FN"><span class="italic" aid="9H6FO">But not yet.</span> She looked up at him. “But you watch that Kennerly. If he doesn’t know a thing, he’ll make it up.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FQ">“Thank you, Allie.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6FR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6FS">When he left she turned to the sink, feeling the hot, warm drift of her grateful tears. How long since anyone had thanked her? Someone who mattered?</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H6FT"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H6FU"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H6FV"><span class="bold1" aid="9H6G0">X</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H6G1"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6G2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6G3">Kennerly was a toothless and unpleasant old satyr who had buried two wives and was plagued with daughters. Two half-grown ones peeked at the gunslinger from the dusty shadows of the barn. A baby drooled happily in the dirt. A full-grown one, blond, dirty, and sensual, watched with a speculative curiosity as she drew water from the groaning pump beside the building. She caught the gunslinger’s eye, pinched her nipples between her fingers, dropped him a wink, and then went back to pumping.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6G4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6G5">The hostler met him halfway between the door to his establishment and the street. His manner vacillated between a kind of hateful hostility and craven fawning.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6G6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6G7">“Hit’s bein’ cared for, never fear ’at,” he said, and before the gunslinger could reply, Kennerly turned on his daughter with his fists up, a desperate scrawny rooster of a man. “You get in, Soobie! You get right the hell in!”</span></p>
<a aid="9H6G8"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6G9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GA">Soobie began to drag her bucket sullenly toward the shack appended to the barn.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GC">“You meant my mule,” the gunslinger said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GE">“Yes, sai. Ain’t seen no mule in quite a time, specially one that looks as threaded as your’n—two eyes, four legs . . .” His face squinched together alarmingly in an expression meant to convey either extreme pain or the notion that a joke had been made. The gunslinger assumed it was the latter, although he had little or no sense of humor himself.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GG">“Time was they used to grow up wild for want of ’em,” Kennerly continued, “but the world has moved on. Ain’t seen nothin’ but a few mutie oxen and the coach horses and—Soobie, I’ll whale you, ’fore God!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GI">“I don’t bite,” the gunslinger said pleasantly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GK">Kennerly cringed and grinned. The gunslinger saw the murder in his eyes quite clearly, and although he did not fear it, he marked it as a man might mark a page in a book, one that contained potentially valuable instructions. “It ain’t <span class="italic" aid="9H6GL">you.</span> Gods, no, it ain’t <span class="italic" aid="9H6GM">you.</span>” He grinned loosely. “She just naturally gawky. She got a devil. She wild.” His eyes darkened. “It’s coming to Last Times, mister. You know how it says in the Book. Children won’t obey their parents, and a plague’ll be visited on the multitudes. You only have to listen to the preacher-woman to know it.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GO">The gunslinger nodded, then pointed southeast. “What’s out there?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GP"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GQ">Kennerly grinned again, showing gums and a few sociable yellow teeth. “Dwellers. Weed. Desert. What else?” He cackled, and his eyes measured the gunslinger coldly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GS">“How big is the desert?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6GT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6GU">“Big.” Kennerly endeavored to look serious, as if answering a serious question. “Maybe a thousand wheels. Maybe two thousand. I can’t tell you, mister. There’s nothin’ out there but devil-grass <a aid="9H6GV"></a>and maybe demons. Heard there was a speakin-ring sommers on the far side, but that ’us prolly a lie. That’s the way the other fella went. The one who fixed up Norty when he was sick.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6H0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6H1">“Sick? I heard he was dead.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6H2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6H3">Kennerly kept grinning. “Well, well. Maybe. But we’re growed-up men, ain’t we?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6H4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6H5">“But you believe in demons.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6H6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6H7">Kennerly looked affronted. “That’s a lot different. Preacher-woman says . . .”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6H8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6H9">He blathered and palavered ever onward. The gunslinger took off his hat and wiped his forehead. The sun was hot, beating steadily. Kennerly seemed not to notice. Kennerly had a lot to say, none of it sensible. In the thin shadow by the livery, the baby girl was gravely smearing dirt on her face.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HA"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HB">The gunslinger finally grew impatient and cut the man off in mid-spate. “You don’t know what’s after the desert?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HC"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HD">Kennerly shrugged. “Some might. The coach ran through part of it fifty years ago. My pap said so. He used to say ’twas mountains. Others say an ocean . . . a green ocean with monsters. And some say that’s where the world ends. That there ain’t nothing but lights that’ll drive a man blind and the face of God with his mouth open to eat them up.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HE"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HF">“Drivel,” the gunslinger said shortly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HG"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HH">“Sure it is,” Kennerly cried happily. He cringed again, hating, fearing, wanting to please.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HJ">“You see my mule is looked after.” He flicked Kennerly another coin, which Kennerly caught on the fly. The gunslinger thought of the way a dog will catch a ball.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HL">“Surely. You stayin’ a little?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HN">“I guess I might. There’ll be water—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HP">“—if God wills it! Sure, sure!” Kennerly laughed unhappily, <a aid="9H6HQ"></a>and his eyes went on wanting the gunslinger stretched out dead at his feet. “That Allie’s pretty nice when she wants to be, ain’t she?” The hostler made a loose circle with his left fist and began poking his right finger rapidly in and out of it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HS">“Did you say something?” the gunslinger asked remotely.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6HU">Sudden terror dawned in Kennerly’s eyes, like twin moons coming over the horizon. He put his hands behind his back like a naughty child caught with the jamjar. “No, sai, not a word. And I’m right sorry if I did.” He caught sight of Soobie leaning out a window and whirled on her. “I’ll whale you now, you little slut-whore! ’Fore God! I’ll—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6HV"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6I0">The gunslinger walked away, aware that Kennerly had turned to watch him, aware of the fact that he could whirl and catch the hostler with some true and untinctured emotion distilled on his face. Why bother? It was hot, and he knew what the emotion would be: just hate. Hate of the outsider. He’d gotten all the man had to offer. The only sure thing about the desert was its size. The only sure thing about the town was that it wasn’t all played out here. Not yet.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H6I1"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H6I2"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H6I3"><span class="bold1" aid="9H6I4">XI</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H6I5"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6I6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6I7">He and Allie were in bed when Sheb kicked the door open and came in with the knife.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6I8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6I9">It had been four days, and they had gone by in a blinking haze. He ate. He slept. He had sex with Allie. He found that she played the fiddle and he made her play it for him. She sat by the window in the milky light of daybreak, only a profile, and played something haltingly that might have been good if she’d had some training. He felt a growing (but strangely absentminded) affection <a aid="9H6IA"></a>for her and thought this might be the trap the man in black had left behind. He walked out sometimes. He thought very little about everything.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IC">He didn’t hear the little piano player come up—his reflexes had sunk. That didn’t seem to matter either, although it would have frightened him badly in another time and place.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6ID"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IE">Allie was naked, the sheet below her breasts, and they were preparing to make love.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IG">“Please,” she was saying. “Like before, I want that, I want—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6II">The door crashed open and the piano player made his ridiculous, knock-kneed run for the sun. Allie did not scream, although Sheb held an eight-inch carving knife in his hand. He was making a noise, an inarticulate blabbering. He sounded like a man being drowned in a bucket of mud. Spittle flew. He brought the knife down with both hands, and the gunslinger caught his wrists and turned them. The knife went flying. Sheb made a high screeching noise, like a rusty screen door. His hands fluttered in marionette movements, both wrists broken. The wind gritted against the window. Allie’s looking glass on the wall, faintly clouded and distorted, reflected the room.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IK">“She was mine!” He wept. “She was mine first! Mine!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IM">Allie looked at him and got out of bed. She put on a wrapper, and the gunslinger felt a moment of empathy for a man who must be seeing himself coming out on the far end of what he once had. He was just a little man. And the gunslinger suddenly knew where he had seen him before. Known him before.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IO">“It was for you,” Sheb sobbed. “It was only for you, Allie. It was you first and it was all for you. I—ah, oh God, dear God . . .” The words dissolved into a paroxysm of unintelligibilities, finally to tears. He rocked back and forth holding his broken wrists to his belly.</span></p>
<a aid="9H6IP"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IR">“Shhh. Shhh. Let me see.” She knelt beside him. “Broken. Sheb, you ass. How will you make your living now? Didn’t you know you were never strong?” She helped him to his feet. He tried to hold his hands to his face, but they would not obey, and he wept nakedly. “Come on over to the table and let me see what I can do.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IT">She led him to the table and set his wrists with slats of kindling from the fire box. He wept weakly and without volition.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6IU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6IV">“Mejis,” the gunslinger said, and the little piano player looked around, eyes wide. The gunslinger nodded, amiably enough now that Sheb was no longer trying to stick a knife in his lights. <span class="italic" aid="9H6J0">“Mejis,”</span> he said again. “On the Clean Sea.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6J1"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6J2">“What about it?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6J3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6J4">“You were there, weren’t you? Many and many-a, as they did say.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6J5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6J6">“What if I was? I don’t remember you.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6J7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6J8">“But you remember the girl, don’t you? The girl named Susan? And Reap night?” His voice took on an edge. “Were you there for the bonfire?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6J9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JA">The little man’s lips trembled. They were covered with spit. His eyes said he knew the truth: he was closer to dead now than when he’d come bursting in with a knife in his hand.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JC">“Get out of here,” the gunslinger said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JE">Understanding dawned in Sheb’s eyes. “But you was just a <span class="italic" aid="9H6JF">boy!</span> One of them three <span class="italic" aid="9H6JG">boys!</span> You come to count stock, and Eldred Jonas was there, the Coffin Hunter, and—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JI">“Get out while you still can,” the gunslinger said, and Sheb went, holding his broken wrists before him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JK">She came back to the bed. “What was that about?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JL"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JM">“Never mind,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JO">“All right—then where were we?”</span></p>
<a aid="9H6JP"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JR">“Nowhere.” He rolled on his side, away from her.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JT">She said patiently, “You knew about him and me. He did what he could, which wasn’t much, and I took what I could, because I had to. There’s nothing to be done. What else is there?” She touched his shoulder. “Except I’m glad that you are so strong.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6JU"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6JV">“Not now,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6K0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6K1">“Who was she?” And then, answering her own question: “A girl you loved.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6K2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6K3">“Leave it, Allie.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6K4"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6K5">“I can make you strong—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6K6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6K7">“No,” he said. “You can’t do that.”</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="9H6K8"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="9H6K9"><span class="calibre9" aid="9H6KA"><span class="bold1" aid="9H6KB">XII</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="9H6KC"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="9H6KD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KE">The next night the bar was closed. It was whatever passed for the Sabbath in Tull. The gunslinger went to the tiny, leaning church by the graveyard while Allie washed tables with strong disinfectant and rinsed kerosene lamp chimneys in soapy water.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6KF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KG">An odd purple dusk had fallen, and the church, lit from the inside, looked almost like a blast furnace from the road.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6KH"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KI">“I don’t go,” Allie had said shortly. “The woman who preaches has poison religion. Let the respectable ones go.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6KJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KK">He stood in the vestibule, hidden in a shadow, looking in. The pews were gone and the congregation stood (he saw Kennerly and his brood; Castner, owner of the town’s scrawny dry-goods emporium and his slat-sided wife; a few barflies; a few “town” women he had never seen before; and, surprisingly, Sheb). They were singing a hymn raggedly, <span class="italic" aid="9H6KL">a cappella.</span> He looked curiously at the mountainous woman at the pulpit. Allie had said: “She lives alone, hardly ever sees anybody. Only comes out on Sunday to <a aid="9H6KM"></a>serve up the hellfire. Her name is Sylvia Pittston. She’s crazy, but she’s got the hoodoo on them. They like it that way. It suits them.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6KN"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KO">No description could take the measure of the woman. Breasts like earthworks. A huge pillar of a neck overtopped by a pasty white moon of a face, in which blinked eyes so large and so dark that they seemed to be bottomless tarns. Her hair was a beautiful rich brown and it was piled atop her head in a haphazard sprawl, held by a hairpin almost big enough to be a meat skewer. She wore a dress that seemed to be made of burlap. The arms that held the hymnal were slabs. Her skin was creamy, unmarked, lovely. He thought that she must top three hundred pounds. He felt a sudden red lust for her that made him feel shaky, and he turned his head and looked away.</span></p>
<blockquote class="calibre17" aid="9H6KP"><p class="calibre_13" aid="9H6KQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KR"><span class="italic" aid="9H6KS">“Shall we gather at the river,</span></span></p><p class="calibre_13" aid="9H6KT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6KU"><span class="italic" aid="9H6KV">The beautiful, the beautiful,</span></span></p><p class="calibre_13" aid="9H6L0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6L1"><span class="italic" aid="9H6L2">The riiiiver,</span></span></p><p class="calibre_13" aid="9H6L3"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6L4"><span class="italic" aid="9H6L5">Shall we gather at the river,</span></span></p><p class="calibre_13" aid="9H6L6"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6L7"><span class="italic" aid="9H6L8">That flows by the kingdom of God.”</span></span></p></blockquote>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6L9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LA">The last note of the last chorus faded off, and there was a moment of shuffling and coughing.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LC">She waited. When they were settled, she spread her hands over them, as if in benediction. It was an evocative gesture.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LE">“My dear little brothers and sisters in Christ.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LG">It was a haunting line. For a moment the gunslinger felt mixed feelings of nostalgia and fear, stitched in with an eerie feeling of <span class="italic" aid="9H6LH">déjà vu,</span> and he thought: <span class="italic" aid="9H6LI">I dreamed this. Or I was here before. If so, when? Not Mejis.</span> No, not there. He shook the feeling off. The audience—perhaps twenty-five all told—had become dead silent. Every eye touched the preacher-woman.</span></p>
<a aid="9H6LJ"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LL">“The subject of our meditation tonight is The Interloper.” Her voice was sweet, melodious, the speaking voice of a well-trained contralto.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LN">A little rustle ran through the audience.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LP">“I feel,” Sylvia Pittston said reflectively, “that I know almost everyone in the Good Book personally. In the last five years I have worn out three of ’em, precious though any book be in this ill world, and uncountable numbers before that. I love the story, and I love the players in that story. I have walked arm in arm in the lion’s den with Daniel. I stood with David when he was tempted by Bathsheba as she bathed at the pool. I have been in the fiery furnace with Shadrach, Meschach, and Abednego. I slew two thousand with Samson when he swung the jawbone, and was blinded with St. Paul on the road to Damascus. I wept with Mary at Golgotha.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LR">A soft, shurring sigh in the audience.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LS"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6LT">“I have known and loved them. There is only <span class="italic" aid="9H6LU">one</span>”—she held up a finger—“only one player in the greatest of all dramas that I do not know.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6LV"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6M0">“Only <span class="italic" aid="9H6M1">one</span> who stands outside with his face in the shadow.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6M2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6M3">“Only <span class="italic" aid="9H6M4">one</span> who makes my body tremble and my spirit quail.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6M5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6M6">“I fear him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6M7"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6M8">“I don’t know his mind and I fear him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6M9"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MA">“I fear The Interloper.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MC">Another sigh. One of the women had put a hand over her mouth as if to stop a sound and was rocking, rocking.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MD"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6ME">“The Interloper who came to Eve as a snake on its belly in the dust, grinning and writhing. The Interloper who walked among the Children of Israel while Moses was up on the Mount, who whispered to them to make a golden idol, a golden calf, and to worship it with foulness and fornication.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MF"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MG">Moans, nods.</span></p>
<a aid="9H6MH"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MI"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MJ">“The Interloper!</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MK"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6ML">“He stood on the balcony with Jezebel and watched as King Ahaz fell screaming to his death, and he and she grinned as the dogs gathered and lapped up his blood. Oh, my little brothers and sisters, watch thou for The Interloper.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MM"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MN">“Yes, O Jesus—” This was the man the gunslinger had first noticed coming into town, the one with the straw hat.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MO"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MP">“He’s always been there, my brothers and sisters. But I don’t know his mind. And <span class="italic" aid="9H6MQ">you</span> don’t know his mind. Who could understand the awful darkness that swirls there, the pride and the titanic blasphemy, the unholy glee? And the madness! The gibbering madness that walks and crawls and wriggles through men’s most awful wants and desires?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MR"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MS">“O Jesus Savior—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6MT"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6MU">“It was <span class="italic" aid="9H6MV">him</span> who took our Lord up on the mountain—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6N0"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6N1">“Yes—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6N2"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6N3">“It was <span class="italic" aid="9H6N4">him</span> that tempted him and shewed him all the world and the world’s pleasures—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6N5"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6N6"><span class="italic" aid="9H6N7">“Yesss—”</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6N8"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6N9">“It’s <span class="italic" aid="9H6NA">him</span> that will return when Last Times come on the world . . . and they are coming, my brothers and sisters, can’t you feel they are?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6NB"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6NC"><span class="italic" aid="9H6ND">“Yesss—”</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="9H6NE"><span class="calibre3" aid="9H6NF">Rocking and sobbing, the congregation became a sea; the woman seemed to point at all of them and none of them.</span></p>
