His father owns stock in a company that makes automatic pin-setting machinery, but Mid-Town Lanes does not use his father’s brand. He does not think he has thought about this, but he has.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE717"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE718"><span class="italic" aid="BE719">Walking down the street, he passes Bloomie’s, where the models stand dressed in fur coats, in six-button Edwardian suits, some in nothing at all; some are “barenaked.” These models—these</span> mannequins<span class="italic" aid="BE71A">—are perfectly professional, and he hates all professionalism. He is too young to have learned to hate himself yet, but that seed is already there; given time, it will grow, and bear bitter fruit.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE71B"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE71C"><span class="italic" aid="BE71D">He comes to the corner and stands with his bookbag at his side. Traffic roars by—grunting blue-and-white busses, yellow taxis, Volkswagens, a large truck. He is just a boy, but not average, and he sees the man who kills him out of the corner of his eye. It is the man in black, and he doesn’t see the face, only the swirling robe, the outstretched hands, and the hard, professional</span>
<a aid="BE71E"></a><span class="italic" aid="BE71F">grin. He falls into the street with his arms outstretched, not letting go of the bookbag which contains Mrs. Greta Shaw’s extremely professional lunch. There is a brief glance through a polarized windshield at the horrified face of a businessman wearing a dark-blue hat in the band of which is a small, jaunty feather. Somewhere a radio is blasting rock and roll. An old woman on the far curb screams—she is wearing a black hat with a net. Nothing jaunty about that black net; it is like a mourner’s veil. Jake feels nothing but surprise and his usual sense of headlong bewilderment—is this how it ends? Before he’s bowled better than two-seventy? He lands hard in the street and looks at an asphalt-sealed crack some two inches from his eyes. The bookbag is jolted from his hand. He is wondering if he has skinned his knees when the car belonging to the businessman wearing the blue hat with the jaunty feather passes over him. It is a big blue 1976 Cadillac with whitewall Firestone tires. The car is almost exactly the same color as the businessman’s hat. It breaks Jake’s back, mushes his guts to gravy, and sends blood from his mouth in a high-pressure jet. He turns his head and sees the Cadillac’s flaming taillights and smoke spurting from beneath its locked rear wheels. The car has also run over his bookbag and left a wide black tread on it. He turns his head the other way and sees a large gray Ford screaming to a stop inches from his body. A black fellow who has been selling pretzels and sodas from a pushcart is coming toward him on the run. Blood runs from Jake’s nose, ears, eyes, rectum. His genitals have been squashed. He wonders irritably how badly he has skinned his knees. He wonders if he’ll be late for school. Now the driver of the Cadillac is running toward him, babbling. Somewhere a terrible, calm voice, the voice of doom, says: “I am a priest. Let me through. An Act of Contrition . . .”</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE71G"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE71H"><span class="italic" aid="BE71I">He sees the black robe and knows sudden horror. It is him, the</span>
<a aid="BE71J"></a><span class="italic" aid="BE71K">man in black. Jake turns his face away with the last of his strength. Somewhere a radio is playing a song by the rock group Kiss. He sees his own hand trailing on the pavement, small, white, shapely. He has never bitten his nails.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE71L"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE71M"><span class="italic" aid="BE71N">Looking at his hand, Jake dies.</span></span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE71O"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE71P"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE71Q"><span class="bold1" aid="BE71R">IV</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE71S"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE71T"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE71U">The gunslinger hunkered in frowning thought. He was tired and his body ached and the thoughts came with aggravating slowness. Across from him the amazing boy slept with his hands folded in his lap, still breathing calmly. He had told his tale without much emotion, although his voice had trembled near the end, when he had come to the part about the “priest” and the “Act of Contrition.” He had not, of course, told the gunslinger about his family and his own sense of bewildered dichotomy, but that had seeped through anyway—enough for the gunslinger to make out its shape. The fact that there had never been such a city as the boy described (unless it was the mythic city of Lud) was not the most upsetting part of the story, but it was disturbing. It was all disturbing. The gunslinger was afraid of the implications.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE71V"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE720">“Jake?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE721"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE722">“Uh-huh?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE723"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE724">“Do you want to remember this when you wake up, or forget it?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE725"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE726">“Forget it,” the boy said promptly. “When the blood came out of my mouth I could taste my own shit.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE727"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE728">“All right. You’re going to sleep, understand? Real sleep now. Go ahead and lie over, if it do please ya.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE729"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72A">Jake laid over, looking small and peaceful and harmless. The <a aid="BE72B"></a>gunslinger did not believe he was harmless. There was a deadly feeling about him, the stink of yet another trap. He didn’t like the feeling, but he liked the boy. He liked him a great deal.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72C"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72D">“Jake?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72E"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72F">“Shhh. I’m sleeping. I want to sleep.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72G"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72H">“Yes. And when you wake up you won’t remember any of this.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72I"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72J">“ ’Kay. Good.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72K"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72L">The gunslinger watched him for a brief time, thinking of his own boyhood, which usually seemed to have happened to another person—to a person who had leaped through some fabulous lens of time to become someone else—but which now seemed poignantly close. It was very hot in the stable of the way station, and he carefully drank some more water. He got up and walked to the back of the building, pausing to look into one of the horse stalls. There was a small pile of white hay in the corner, and a neatly folded blanket, but there was no smell of horse. There was no smell of anything in the stable. The sun had bled away every smell and left nothing. The air was perfectly neutral.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72M"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72N">At the back of the stable was a small, dark room with a stainless steel machine in the center. It was untouched by rust or rot. It looked like a butter churn. At the left, a chrome pipe jutted from it, terminating over a drain in the floor. The gunslinger had seen pumps like it in other dry places, but never one so big. He could not contemplate how deep they (some long-gone they) must have drilled before they struck water, secret and forever black under the desert.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72O"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72P">Why hadn’t they removed the pump when the way station had been abandoned?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72Q"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72R">Demons, perhaps.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72S"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE72T">He shuddered abruptly, an abrupt twisting of his back. <a aid="BE72U"></a>Heat-flesh poked out on his skin, then receded. He went to the control switch and pushed the ON button. The machine began to hum. After perhaps half a minute, a stream of cool, clear water belched from the pipe and went down the drain to be recirculated. Perhaps three gallons flowed out of the pipe before the pump shut itself down with a final click. It was a thing as alien to this place and time as true love, and yet as concrete as a Judgment, a silent reminder of the time when the world had not yet moved on. It probably ran on an atomic slug, as there was no electricity within a thousand miles of here and even dry batteries would have lost their charge long ago. It had been made by a company called North Central Positronics. The gunslinger didn’t like it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE72V"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE730">He went back and sat down beside the boy, who had put one hand under his cheek. Nice-looking boy. The gunslinger drank some more water and crossed his legs so he was sitting Indian fashion. The boy, like the squatter on the edge of the desert who kept the bird (Zoltan, the gunslinger remembered abruptly, the bird’s name was Zoltan), had lost his sense of time, but the fact that the man in black was closer seemed beyond doubt. Not for the first time, the gunslinger wondered if the man in black was letting him catch up for some reason of his own. Perhaps the gunslinger was playing into his hands. He tried to imagine what the confrontation might be like, and could not.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE731"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE732">He was very hot, but no longer felt sick. The nursery rhyme occurred to him again, but this time instead of his mother, he thought of Cort—Cort, an ageless engine of a man, his face stitched with the scars of bricks and bullets and blunt instruments. The scars of war and instruction in the arts of war. He wondered if Cort had ever had a love to match those monumental scars. He doubted it. He thought of Susan, and his mother, and of Marten, that incomplete enchanter.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE733"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE734">The gunslinger was not a man to dwell on the past; only a <a aid="BE735"></a>shadowy conception of the future and of his own emotional make-up saved him from being a man without imagination, a dangerous dullard. His present run of thought therefore rather amazed him. Each name called up others—Cuthbert, Alain, the old man Jonas with his quavery voice; and again Susan, the lovely girl at the window. Such thoughts always came back to Susan, and the great rolling plain known as the Drop, and fishermen casting their nets in the bays on the edge of the Clean Sea.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE736"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE737">The piano player in Tull (also dead, all dead in Tull, and by his hand) had known those places, although he and the gunslinger had only spoken of them that once. Sheb had been fond of the old songs, had once played them in a saloon called the Traveller’s Rest, and the gunslinger hummed one tunelessly under his breath:</span></p>
<blockquote class="calibre17" aid="BE738"><p class="calibre_13" aid="BE739"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73A"><span class="italic" aid="BE73B">Love o love o careless love</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_13" aid="BE73C"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73D"><span class="italic" aid="BE73E">See what careless love has done.</span></span></p></blockquote>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE73F"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73G">The gunslinger laughed, bemused. <span class="italic" aid="BE73H">I am the last of that green and warm-hued world.</span> And for all his nostalgia, he felt no self-pity. The world had moved on mercilessly, but his legs were still strong, and the man in black was closer. The gunslinger nodded out.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE73I"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE73J"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE73K"><span class="bold1" aid="BE73L">V</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE73M"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE73N"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73O">When he awoke, it was almost dark and the boy was gone.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE73P"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73Q">The gunslinger got up, hearing his joints pop, and went to the stable door. There was a small flame dancing in darkness on the porch of the inn. He walked toward it, his shadow long and black and trailing in the reddish-ochre light of sunset.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE73R"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73S">Jake was sitting by a kerosene lamp. “The oil was in a drum,” <a aid="BE73T"></a>he said, “but I was scared to burn it in the house. Everything’s so dry—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE73U"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE73V">“You did just right.” The gunslinger sat down, seeing but not thinking about the dust of years that puffed up around his rump. He thought it something of a wonder that the porch didn’t simply collapse beneath their combined weight. The flame from the lamp shadowed the boy’s face with delicate tones. The gunslinger produced his poke and rolled a cigarette.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE740"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE741">“We have to palaver,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE742"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE743">Jake nodded, smiling a little at the word.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE744"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE745">“I guess you know I’m on the prod for that man you saw.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE746"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE747">“Are you going to kill him?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE748"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE749">“I don’t know. I have to make him tell me something. I may have to make him take me someplace.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74A"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74B">“Where?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74C"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74D">“To find a tower,” the gunslinger said. He held his cigarette over the chimney of the lamp and drew on it; the smoke drifted away on the rising night breeze. Jake watched it. His face showed neither fear nor curiosity, certainly not enthusiasm.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74E"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74F">“So I’m going on tomorrow,” the gunslinger said. “You’ll have to come with me. How much of that meat is left?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74G"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74H">“Only a little.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74I"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74J">“Corn?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74K"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74L">“A little more.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74M"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74N">The gunslinger nodded. “Is there a cellar?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74O"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74P">“Yes.” Jake looked at him. The pupils of his eyes had grown to a huge, fragile size. “You pull up on a ring in the floor, but I didn’t go down. I was afraid the ladder would break and I wouldn’t be able to get up again. And it smells bad. It’s the only thing around here that smells at all.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74Q"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74R">“We’ll get up early and see if there’s anything down there worth taking. Then we’ll go.”</span></p>
<a aid="BE74S"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74T"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE74U">“All right.” The boy paused and then said, “I’m glad I didn’t kill you when you were sleeping. I had a pitchfork and I thought about doing it. But I didn’t, and now I won’t have to be afraid to go to sleep.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE74V"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE750">“What would you be afraid of?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE751"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE752">The boy looked at him ominously. “Spooks. Of <span class="italic" aid="BE753">him</span> coming back.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE754"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE755">“The man in black,” the gunslinger said. Not a question.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE756"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE757">“Yes. Is he a bad man?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE758"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE759">“I guess that depends on where you’re standing,” the gunslinger said absently. He got up and pitched his cigarette out onto the hardpan. “I’m going to sleep.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75A"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75B">The boy looked at him timidly. “Can I sleep in the stable with you?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75C"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75D">“Of course.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75E"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75F">The gunslinger stood on the steps, looking up, and the boy joined him. Old Star was up there, and Old Mother. It seemed to the gunslinger that if he closed his eyes he would be able to hear the croaking of the first spring peepers, smell the green, almost-summer smell of the court lawns after their first cutting (and hear, perhaps, the indolent click of wooden balls as the ladies of the East Wing, attired only in their shifts as dusk glimmered toward dark, played at Points), could almost see Cuthbert and Jamie as they came through the break in the hedges, calling for him to ride out with them . . .</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75G"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75H">It was not like him to think so much of the past.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75I"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75J">He turned back and picked up the lamp. “Let’s go to sleep,” he said.</span></p><p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75K"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75L">They crossed to the stable together.</span></p><a aid="BE75M"></a>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE75N"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE75O"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE75P"><span class="bold1" aid="BE75Q">VI</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE75R"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE75S"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75T"> The next morning he explored the cellar.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE75U"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE75V">Jake was right; it smelled bad. It had a wet, swampy stench that made the gunslinger feel nauseous and a little lightheaded after the antiseptic odorlessness of the desert and the stable. The cellar smelled of cabbages and turnips and potatoes with long, sightless eyes gone to everlasting rot. The ladder, however, seemed quite sturdy, and he climbed down.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE760"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE761">The floor was earthen, and his head almost touched the overhead beams. Down here spiders still lived, disturbingly big ones with mottled gray bodies. Many were muties, the true thread long-lost. Some had eyes on stalks, some had what might have been as many as sixteen legs.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE762"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE763">The gunslinger peered around and waited for his nighteyes.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE764"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE765">“You all right?” Jake called down nervously.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE766"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE767">“Yes.” He focused on the corner. “There are cans. Wait.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE768"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE769">He went carefully to the corner, ducking his head. There was an old box with one side folded down. The cans were vegetables—green beans, yellow beans—and three cans of corned beef.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76A"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76B">He scooped up an armload and went back to the ladder. He climbed halfway up and handed them to Jake, who knelt to receive them. He went back for more.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76C"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76D">It was on the third trip that he heard the groaning in the foundations.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76E"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76F">He turned, looked, and felt a kind of dreamy terror wash over him, a feeling both languid and repellent.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76G"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76H">The foundation was composed of huge sandstone blocks that had probably been evenly cornered when the way station was new, but which were now at every zigzag, drunken angle. It made the wall look as if it were inscribed with strange, meandering <a aid="BE76I"></a>hieroglyphics. And from the joining of two of these abstruse cracks, a thin spill of sand was running, as if something on the other side was digging itself through with slobbering, agonized intensity.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76J"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76K">The groaning rose and fell, becoming louder, until the whole cellar was full of the sound, an abstract noise of ripping pain and dreadful effort.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76L"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76M">“Come up!” Jake screamed. “Oh Jesus, mister, come up!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76N"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76O">“Go away,” the gunslinger said calmly. “Wait outside. If I don’t come up by the time you count to two . . . no, three hundred, get the hell out.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76P"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76Q"><span class="italic" aid="BE76R">“Come up!”</span> Jake screamed again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76S"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76T">The gunslinger didn’t answer. He pulled leather with his right hand.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE76U"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE76V">There was a hole as big as a coin in the wall now. He could hear, through the curtain of his own terror, Jake’s pattering feet as the boy ran. Then the spill of sand stopped. The groaning ceased, but there was a sound of steady, labored breathing.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE770"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE771">“Who are you?” the gunslinger asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE772"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE773">No answer.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE774"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE775">And in the High Speech, his voice filling with the old thunder of command, Roland demanded: “Who are you, Demon? Speak, if you would speak. My time is short; my patience shorter.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE776"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE777">“Go slow,” a dragging, clotted voice said from within the wall. And the gunslinger felt the dream-like terror deepen and grow almost solid. It was the voice of Alice, the woman he had stayed with in the town of Tull. But she was dead; he had seen her go down himself, a bullet hole between her eyes. Fathoms seemed to swim by his eyes, descending. “Go slow past the Drawers, gunslinger. Watch for the taheen. While you travel with the boy, the man in black travels with your soul in his pocket.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE778"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE779">“What do you mean? Speak on!”</span></p>
<a aid="BE77A"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77B"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77C">But the breathing was gone.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77D"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77E">The gunslinger stood for a moment, frozen, and then one of the huge spiders dropped on his arm and scrambled frantically up to his shoulder. With an involuntary grunt he brushed it away and got his feet moving. He did not want to do the next thing, but custom was strict, inviolable. Take the dead from the dead, the old proverb said; only a corpse may speak true prophecy. He went to the hole and punched at it. The sandstone crumbled easily at the edges, and with a bare stiffening of muscles, he thrust his hand through the wall.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77F"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77G">And touched something solid, with raised and fretted knobs. He drew it out. He held a jawbone, rotted at the far hinge. The teeth leaned this way and that.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77H"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77I">“All right,” he said softly. He thrust it rudely into his back pocket and went back up the ladder, carrying the last cans awkwardly. He left the trapdoor open. The sun would get in and kill the mutie spiders.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77J"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77K">Jake was halfway across the stable yard, cowering on the cracked, rubbly hardpan. He screamed when he saw the gunslinger, backed away a step or two, and then ran to him, crying.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77L"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77M">“I thought it got you, that it got you. I thought—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77N"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77O">“It didn’t. Nothing got me.” He held the boy to him, feeling his face, hot against his chest, and his hands, dry against his ribcage. He could feel the rapid beating of the boy’s heart. It occurred to him later that this was when he began to love the boy—which was, of course, what the man in black must have planned all along. Was there ever a trap to match the trap of love?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77P"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77Q">“Was it a demon?” The voice was muffled.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77R"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77S">“Yes. A speaking-demon. We don’t have to go back there anymore. Come on. Let’s shake a mile.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE77T"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE77U">They went to the stable, and the gunslinger made a rough <a aid="BE77V"></a>pack from the blanket he’d slept under—it was hot and prickly, but there was nothing else. That done, he filled the waterbags from the pump.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE780"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE781">“You carry one of the waterbags,” the gunslinger said. “Wear it around your shoulders—see?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE782"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE783">“Yes.” The boy looked up at him worshipfully, the look quickly masked. He slung one of the bags over his shoulders.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE784"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE785">“Is it too heavy?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE786"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE787">“No. It’s fine.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE788"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE789">“Tell me the truth, now. I can’t carry you if you get a sunstroke.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78A"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78B">“I won’t have a sunstroke. I’ll be okay.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78C"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78D">The gunslinger nodded.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78E"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78F">“We’re going to the mountains, aren’t we?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78G"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78H">“Yes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78I"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78J">They walked out into the steady smash of the sun. Jake, his head as high as the swing of the gunslinger’s elbows, walked to his right and a little ahead, the rawhide-wrapped ends of the waterbag hanging nearly to his shins. The gunslinger had crisscrossed two more waterbags across his shoulders and carried the sling of food in his armpit, his left arm holding it against his body. In his right hand was his pack, his poke, and the rest of his gunna.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78K"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78L">They passed through the far gate of the way station and found the blurred ruts of the stage track again. They had walked perhaps fifteen minutes when Jake turned around and waved at the two buildings. They seemed to huddle in the titanic space of the desert.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78M"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78N">“Goodbye!” Jake cried. “Goodbye!” Then he turned back to the gunslinger, looking troubled. “I feel like something’s watching us.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_" id="filepos268375" aid="BE78O"><a href="kindle:pos:fid:0003:off:000000003Q" aid="BE78P"><img alt="thegunsling_98.jpg" class="calibre13" src="kindle:embed:0005?mime=image/jpeg"/></a></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78Q"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78R">“Something or someone,” the gunslinger agreed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78S"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78T">“Was someone hiding there? Hiding there all along?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE78U"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE78V">“I don’t know. I don’t think so.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE790"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE791">“Should we go back? Go back and—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE792"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE793">“No. We’re done with that place.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE794"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE795">“Good,” Jake said fervently.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE796"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE797">They walked. The stage track breasted a frozen sand drumlin, and when the gunslinger looked around, the way station was gone. Once again there was the desert, and that only.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE798"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE799"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE79A"><span class="bold1" aid="BE79B">VII</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE79C"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE79D"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79E">They were three days out of the way station; the mountains were deceptively clear now. They could see the smooth, stepped rise of the desert into foothills, the first naked slopes, the bedrock bursting through the skin of the earth in sullen, eroded triumph. Further up, the land gentled off briefly again, and for the first time in months or years the gunslinger could see real, living green. Grass, dwarf spruces, perhaps even willows, all fed by snow runoff from further up. Beyond that the rock took over again, rising in cyclopean, tumbled splendor all the way to the blinding snowcaps. Off to the left, a huge slash showed the way to the smaller, eroded sandstone cliffs and mesas and buttes on the far side. This draw was obscured in the almost continual gray membrane of showers. At night, Jake would sit fascinated for the few minutes before he fell into sleep, watching the brilliant swordplay of the far-off lightning, white and purple, startling in the clarity of the night air.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79F"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79G">The boy was fine on the trail. He was tough, but more than that, he seemed to fight exhaustion with a calm reservoir of will <a aid="BE79H"></a>which the gunslinger appreciated and admired. He didn’t talk much and he didn’t ask questions, not even about the jawbone, which the gunslinger turned over and over in his hands during his evening smoke. He caught a sense that the boy felt highly flattered by the gunslinger’s companionship—perhaps even exalted by it—and this disturbed him. The boy had been placed in his path—<span class="italic" aid="BE79I">While you travel with the boy, the man in black travels with your soul in his pocket</span>—and the fact that Jake was not slowing him down only opened the way to more sinister possibilities.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79J"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79K">They passed the symmetrical campfire leavings of the man in black at regular intervals, and it seemed to the gunslinger that these leavings were much fresher now. On the third night, the gunslinger was sure that he could see the distant spark of another campfire, somewhere in the first rising swell of the foothills. This did not please him as much as he once might have believed. One of Cort’s sayings occurred to him: <span class="italic" aid="BE79L">’Ware the man who fakes a limp.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79M"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79N">Near two o’clock on the fourth day out from the way station, Jake reeled and almost fell.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79O"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79P">“Here, sit down,” the gunslinger said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79Q"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79R">“No, I’m okay.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79S"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79T">“Sit down.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE79U"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE79V">The boy sat obediently. The gunslinger squatted close by, so Jake would be in his shadow.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7A0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7A1">“Drink.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7A2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7A3">“I’m not supposed to until—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7A4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7A5">“Drink.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7A6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7A7">The boy drank, three swallows. The gunslinger wet the tail of the blanket, which held a good deal less now, and applied the damp fabric to the boy’s wrists and forehead, which were fever-dry.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7A8"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7A9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AA">“From now on we rest every afternoon at this time. Fifteen minutes. Do you want to sleep?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AC">“No.” The boy looked at him with shame. The gunslinger looked back blandly. In an abstracted way he withdrew one of the bullets from his belt and began to twirl it howken between his fingers. The boy watched, fascinated.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AD"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AE">“That’s neat,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AG">The gunslinger nodded. “Yar!” He paused. “When I was your age, I lived in a walled city, did I tell you that?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AI">The boy shook his head sleepily.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AK">“Sure. And there was an evil man—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AL"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AM">“The priest?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AN"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AO">“Well, sometimes I wonder about that, tell you true,” the gunslinger said. “If they <span class="italic" aid="BE7AP">were</span> two, I think now they must have been brothers. Maybe even twins. But did I ever see ’em together? No, I never did. This bad man . . . this Marten . . . he was a wizard. Like Merlin. Do they ken Merlin where you come from?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AR">“Merlin and Arthur and the knights of the Round Table,” Jake said dreamily.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AS"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AT">The gunslinger felt a nasty jolt go through him. “Yes,” he said. “Arthur Eld, you say true, I say thank ya. I was very young . . .”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7AU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7AV">But the boy was asleep sitting up, his hands folded neatly in his lap.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7B0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7B1">“Jake.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7B2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7B3">“Yar!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7B4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7B5">The sound of this word from the boy’s mouth startled him badly, but the gunslinger wouldn’t let his voice show it. “When I snap my fingers, you’ll wake up. You’ll be rested and fresh. Do you kennit?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7B6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7B7">“Yes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7B8"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7B9">“Lie over, then.”</span></p>
<a aid="BE7BA"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7BB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BC">The gunslinger got makings from his poke and rolled a cigarette. There was something missing. He searched for it in his diligent, careful way and located it. The missing thing was his previous maddening sense of hurry, the feeling that he might be left behind at any time, that the trail would die out and he would be left with only a last fading footprint. All that was gone now, and the gunslinger was slowly becoming sure that the man in black wanted to be caught. <span class="italic" aid="BE7BD">’Ware the man who fakes a limp.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7BE"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BF">What would follow?</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7BG"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BH">The question was too vague to catch his interest. Cuthbert would have found interest in it, lively interest (and probably a joke), but Cuthbert was as gone as the Horn o’ Deschain, and the gunslinger could only go forward in the way he knew.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7BI"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BJ">He watched the boy as he smoked, and his mind turned back on Cuthbert, who had always laughed (to his death he had gone laughing), and Cort, who never laughed, and on Marten, who sometimes smiled—a thin, silent smile that had its own disquieting gleam . . . like an eye that slips open in the dark and discloses blood. And there had been the falcon, of course. The falcon was named David, after the legend of the boy with the sling. David, he was quite sure, knew nothing but the need for murder, rending, and terror. Like the gunslinger himself. David was no dilettante; he played the center of the court.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7BK"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BL">Except maybe at the end.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7BM"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BN">The gunslinger’s stomach seemed to rise painfully against his heart, but his face didn’t change. He watched the smoke of his cigarette rise into the hot desert air and disappear, and his mind went back.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7BO"></a>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE7BP"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE7BQ"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE7BR"><span class="bold1" aid="BE7BS">VIII</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE7BT"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE7BU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7BV">The sky was white, perfectly white, the smell of rain strong in the air. The smell of hedges and growing green was sweet. It was deep spring, what some called New Earth.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7C0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7C1">David sat on Cuthbert’s arm, a small engine of destruction with bright golden eyes that glared outward at nothing. The rawhide leash attached to his jesses was looped carelessly about Bert’s arm.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7C2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7C3">Cort stood aside from the two boys, a silent figure in patched leather trousers and a green cotton shirt that had been cinched high with his old, wide infantry belt. The green of his shirt merged with the hedges and the rolling turf of the Back Courts, where the ladies had not yet begun to play at Points.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7C4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7C5">“Get ready,” Roland whispered to Cuthbert.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7C6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7C7">“We’re ready,” Cuthbert said confidently. “Aren’t we, Davey?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7C8"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7C9">They spoke the low speech, the language of both scullions and squires; the day when they would be allowed to use their own tongue in the presence of others was still far. “It’s a beautiful day for it. Can you smell the rain? It’s—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CA"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CB">Cort abruptly raised the trap in his hands and let the side fall open. The dove was out and up, trying for the sky in a quick, fluttering blast of its wings. Cuthbert pulled the leash, but he was slow; the hawk was already up and his takeoff was awkward. The hawk recovered with a brief twitch of its wings. It struck upward, trudging the air, gaining altitude over the dove, moving bullet-swift.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CC"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CD">Cort walked over to where the boys stood, casually, and swung his huge and twisted fist at Cuthbert’s ear. The boy fell over without a sound, although his lips writhed back from his gums. A trickle of blood flowed slowly from his ear and onto the rich green grass.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7CE"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CG">“You were slow, maggot,” he said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CI">Cuthbert was struggling to his feet. “I cry your pardon, Cort. It’s just that I—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CK">Cort swung again, and Cuthbert fell over again. The blood flowed more swiftly now.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CL"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CM">“Speak the High Speech,” he said softly. His voice was flat, with a slight, drunken rasp. “Speak your Act of Contrition in the speech of civilization for which better men than you will ever be have died, maggot.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CN"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CO">Cuthbert was getting up again. Tears stood brightly in his eyes, but his lips were pressed together in a tight line of hate which did not quiver.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CP"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CQ">“I grieve,” Cuthbert said in a voice of breathless control. “I have forgotten the face of my father, whose guns I hope someday to bear.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CR"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CS">“That’s right, brat,” Cort said. “You’ll consider what you did wrong, and sharpen your reflections with hunger. No supper. No breakfast.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CT"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7CU">“Look!” Roland cried. He pointed up.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7CV"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7D0">The hawk had climbed above the soaring dove. It glided for a moment, its stubby wings outstretched and without movement on the still, white spring air. Then it folded its wings and dropped like a stone. The two bodies came together, and for a moment Roland fancied he could see blood in the air. The hawk gave a brief scream of triumph. The dove fluttered, twisting, to the ground, and Roland ran toward the kill, leaving Cort and the chastened Cuthbert behind him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7D1"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7D2">The hawk had landed beside its prey and was complacently tearing into its plump white breast. A few feathers seesawed slowly downward.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7D3"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7D4">“David!” the boy yelled, and tossed the hawk a piece of rabbit flesh from his poke. The hawk caught it on the fly, ingested it with <a aid="BE7D5"></a>an upward shaking of its back and throat, and Roland attempted to re-leash the bird.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7D6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7D7">The hawk whirled, almost absentmindedly, and ripped skin from Roland’s arm in a long, dangling gash. Then it went back to its meal.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7D8"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7D9">With a grunt, Roland looped the leash again, this time catching David’s diving, slashing beak on the leather gauntlet he wore. He gave the hawk another piece of meat, then hooded it. Docilely, David climbed onto his wrist.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DA"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DB">He stood up proudly, the hawk on his arm.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DC"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DD">“What’s this, can ya tell me?” Cort asked, pointing to the dripping slash on Roland’s forearm. The boy stationed himself to receive the blow, locking his throat against any possible cry, but no blow fell.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DE"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DF">“He struck me,” Roland said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DG"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DH">“You pissed him off,” Cort said. “The hawk does not fear you, boy, and the hawk never will. The hawk is God’s gunslinger.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DI"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DJ">Roland merely looked at Cort. He was not an imaginative boy, and if Cort had intended to imply a moral, it was lost on him; he went so far as to believe that it might have been one of the few foolish statements he had ever heard Cort make.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DK"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DL">Cuthbert came up behind them and stuck his tongue out at Cort, safely on his blind side. Roland did not smile, but nodded to him.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DM"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DN">“Go in now,” Cort said, taking the hawk. He turned and pointed at Cuthbert. “But remember your reflection, maggot. And your fast. Tonight and tomorrow morning.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DO"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DP">“Yes,” Cuthbert said, stiltedly formal now. “Thank you for this instructive day.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DR">“You learn,” Cort said, “but your tongue has a bad habit of lolling from your stupid mouth when your instructor’s back is turned. Mayhap the day will come when it and you will learn <a aid="BE7DS"></a>their respective places.” He struck Cuthbert again, this time solidly between the eyes and hard enough so that Roland heard a dull thud—the sound a mallet makes when a scullion taps a keg of beer. Cuthbert fell backward onto the lawn, his eyes cloudy and dazed at first. Then they cleared and he stared burningly up at Cort, his usual easy grin nowhere to be seen, his hatred unveiled, a pinprick as bright as the dove’s blood in the center of each eye. He nodded and parted his lips in a scarifying smile that Roland had never seen.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DT"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7DU">“Then there’s hope for you,” Cort said. “When you think you can, you come for me, maggot.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7DV"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7E0">“How did you know?” Cuthbert said between his teeth.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7E1"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7E2">Cort turned toward Roland so swiftly that Roland almost fell back a step—and then both of them would have been on the grass, decorating the new green with their blood. “I saw it reflected in this maggot’s eyes,” he said. “Remember it, Cuthbert Allgood. Last lesson for today.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7E3"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7E4">Cuthbert nodded again, the same frightening smile on his face. “I grieve,” he said. “I have forgotten the face—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7E5"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7E6">“Cut that shit,” Cort said, losing interest. He turned to Roland. “Go on, now. The both of you. If I have to look at your stupid maggot faces any longer I’ll puke my guts and lose a good dinner.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7E7"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7E8">“Come on,” Roland said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7E9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EA">Cuthbert shook his head to clear it and got to his feet. Cort was already walking down the hill in his squat, bowlegged stride, looking powerful and somehow prehistoric. The shaved and grizzled spot at the top of his head glimmered.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7EB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EC">“I’ll kill the son of a bitch,” Cuthbert said, still smiling. A large goose egg, purple and knotted, was rising mystically on his forehead.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7ED"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EE">“Not you or me,” Roland said, suddenly bursting into a grin. <a aid="BE7EF"></a>“You can have supper in the west kitchen with me. Cook will give us some.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7EG"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EH">“He’ll tell Cort.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7EI"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EJ">“He’s no friend of Cort’s,” Roland said, and then shrugged. “And what if he did?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7EK"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EL">Cuthbert grinned back. “Sure. Right. I always wanted to know how the world looked when your head was on backwards and upside down.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7EM"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EN">They started back together over the green lawns, casting shadows in the fine white springlight.</span></p>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE7EO"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE7EP"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE7EQ"><span class="bold1" aid="BE7ER">IX</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE7ES"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE7ET"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7EU">The cook in the west kitchen was named Hax. He stood huge in foodstained whites, a man with a crude-oil complexion whose ancestry was a quarter black, a quarter yellow, a quarter from the South Islands, now almost forgotten (the world had moved on), and a quarter gods-knew-what. He shuffled about three high-ceilinged steamy rooms like a tractor in low gear, wearing huge, Caliph-like slippers. He was one of those quite rare adults who communicate with small children fairly well and who love them all impartially—not in a sugary way but in a business-like fashion that may sometimes entail a hug, in the same way that closing a big business deal may call for a handshake. He even loved the boys who had begun the way of the gun, although they were different from other children—undemonstrative and always slightly dangerous, not in an adult way, but rather as if they were ordinary children with a slight touch of madness—and Bert was not the first of Cort’s students whom he had fed on the sly. At this moment he stood in front of his huge, rambling electric stove—one <a aid="BE7EV"></a>of six working appliances left on the whole estate. It was his personal domain, and he stood there watching the two boys bolt the gravied meat scraps he had produced. Behind, before, and all around, cookboys, scullions, and various underlings rushed through the steaming, humid air, rattling pans, stirring stew, slaving over potatoes and vegetables in nether regions. In the dimly lit pantry alcove, a washerwoman with a doughy, miserable face and hair caught up in a rag splashed water around on the floor with a mop.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7F0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7F1">One of the scullery boys rushed up with a man from the Guards in tow. “This man, he wantchoo, Hax.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7F2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7F3">“All right.” Hax nodded to the Guard, and he nodded back. “You boys,” he said. “Go over to Maggie, she’ll give you some pie. Then scat. Don’t get me in trouble.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7F4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7F5">Later they would both remember he’d said that: <span class="italic" aid="BE7F6">Don’t get me in trouble.</span></span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7F7"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7F8">They nodded and went over to Maggie, who gave them huge wedges of pie on dinner plates—but gingerly, as if they were wild dogs that might bite her.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7F9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FA">“Let’s eat it understairs,” Cuthbert said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FC">“All right.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FD"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FE">They sat behind a huge, sweating stone colonnade, out of sight of the kitchen, and gobbled their pie with their fingers. It was only moments later that they saw shadows fall on the far curving wall of the wide staircase. Roland grabbed Cuthbert’s arm. “Come on,” he said. “Someone’s coming.” Cuthbert looked up, his face surprised and berry-stained.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FG">But the shadows stopped, still out of sight. It was Hax and the man from the Guards. The boys sat where they were. If they moved now, they might be heard.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FI">“. . . the good man,” the Guard was saying.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7FJ"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FK"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FL">“Farson?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FM"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FN">“In two weeks,” the Guard replied. “Maybe three. You have to come with us. There’s a shipment from the freight depot . . .” A particularly loud crash of pots and pans and a volley of catcalls directed at the hapless potboy who had dropped them blotted out some of the rest; then the boys heard the Guard finish: “. . . poisoned meat.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FO"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FP">“Risky.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FR">“Ask not what the good man can do for you—” the Guard began.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FS"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FT">“But what you can do for him.” Hax sighed. “Soldier, ask not.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7FU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7FV">“You know what it could mean,” the Guard said quietly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7G0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7G1">“Yar. And I know my responsibilities to him; you don’t need to lecture me. I love him just as you do. Would foller him into the sea if he asked; so I would.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7G2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7G3">“All right. The meat will be marked for short-term storage in your coldrooms. But you’ll have to be quick. You must understand that.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7G4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7G5">“There are children in Taunton?” the cook asked. It was not really a question.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7G6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7G7">“Children everywhere,” the Guard said gently. “It’s the children we—and he—care about.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7G8"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7G9">“Poisoned meat. Such a strange way to care for children.” Hax uttered a heavy, whistling sigh. “Will they curdle and hold their bellies and cry for their mammas? I suppose they will.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GA"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GB">“It will be like going to sleep,” the Guard said, but his voice was too confidently reasonable.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GC"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GD">“Of course,” Hax said, and laughed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GE"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GF">“You said it yourself. ‘Soldier, ask not.’ Do you enjoy seeing children under the rule of the gun, when they could be under his hands, ready to start making a new world?”</span></p>
<a aid="BE7GG"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GI">Hax did not reply.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GK">“I go on duty in twenty minutes,” the Guard said, his voice once more calm. “Give me a joint of mutton and I’ll pinch one of your girls and make her giggle. When I leave—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GL"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GM">“My mutton will give no cramps to your belly, Robeson.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GN"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GO">“Will you . . .” But the shadows moved away and the voices were lost.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GP"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GQ"><span class="italic" aid="BE7GR">I could have killed them,</span> Roland thought, frozen and fascinated. <span class="italic" aid="BE7GS">I could have killed them both with my knife, slit their throats like hogs.</span> He looked at his hands, now stained with gravy and berries as well as dirt from the day’s lessons.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GT"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7GU">“Roland.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7GV"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7H0">He looked at Cuthbert. They looked at each other for a long moment in the fragrant semidarkness, and a taste of warm despair rose in Roland’s throat. What he felt might have been a sort of death—something as brutal and final as the death of the dove in the white sky over the games field. <span class="italic" aid="BE7H1">Hax?</span> he thought, bewildered. <span class="italic" aid="BE7H2">Hax who put a poultice on my leg that time? Hax?</span> And then his mind snapped closed, cutting the subject off.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7H3"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7H4">What he saw, even in Cuthbert’s humorous, intelligent face, was nothing—nothing at all. Cuthbert’s eyes were flat with Hax’s doom. In Cuthbert’s eyes, it had already happened. He had fed them and they had gone understairs to eat and then Hax had brought the Guard named Robeson to the wrong corner of the kitchen for their treasonous little <span class="italic" aid="BE7H5">tete-a-tete.</span> Ka had worked as ka sometimes did, as suddenly as a big stone rolling down a hillside. That was all.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7H6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7H7">Cuthbert’s eyes were gunslinger’s eyes.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7H8"></a>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE7H9"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE7HA"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE7HB"><span class="bold1" aid="BE7HC">X</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE7HD"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE7HE"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HF">Roland’s father was only just back from the uplands, and he looked out of place amid the drapes and the chiffon fripperies of the main receiving hall to which the boy had only lately been granted access, as a sign of his apprenticeship.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HG"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HH">Steven Deschain was dressed in black jeans and a blue work shirt. His cloak, dusty and streaked, torn to the lining in one place, was slung carelessly over his shoulder with no regard for the way it and he clashed with the elegance of the room. He was desperately thin and the heavy handlebar mustache below his nose seemed to weight his head as he looked down at his son. The guns crisscrossed over the wings of his hips hung at the perfect angle for his hands, the worn sandalwood grips looking dull and sleepy in this languid indoor light.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HI"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HJ">“The head cook,” his father said softly. “Imagine it! The tracks that were blown upland at the railhead. The dead stock in Hendrickson. And perhaps even . . . imagine! Imagine!”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HK"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HL">He looked more closely at his son. “It preys on you.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HM"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HN">“Like the hawk,” Roland said. “It preys on you.” He laughed—at the startling appropriateness of the image rather than at any lightness in the situation.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HO"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HP">His father smiled.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HR">“Yes,” Roland said. “I guess it . . . it preys on me.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HS"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HT">“Cuthbert was with you,” his father said. “He will have told his father by now.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7HU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7HV">“Yes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7I0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7I1">“He fed both of you when Cort—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7I2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7I3">“Yes.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7I4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7I5">“And Cuthbert. Does it prey on him, do you think?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7I6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7I7">“I don’t know.” Nor did he care. He was not concerned with how his feelings compared with those of others.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7I8"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7I9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IA">“It preys on you because you feel you’ve caused a man’s death?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IC">Roland shrugged unwillingly, all at once not content with this probing of his motivations.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7ID"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IE">“Yet you told. Why?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IG">The boy’s eyes widened. “How could I not? Treason was—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7II">His father waved a hand curtly. “If you did it for something as cheap as a schoolbook idea, you did it unworthily. I would rather see all of Taunton poisoned.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IK">“I didn’t!” The words jerked out of him violently. “I wanted to kill him—both of them! Liars! <span class="italic" aid="BE7IL">Black</span> liars! Snakes! They—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IM"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IN">“Go ahead.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IO"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IP">“They hurt me,” he finished, defiant. “They changed something and it hurt. I wanted to kill them for it. I wanted to kill them right there.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IR">His father nodded. “That’s crude, Roland, but not unworthy. Not moral, either, but it is not your place to be moral. In fact . . .” He peered at his son. “Morals may always be beyond you. You are not quick, like Cuthbert or Vannay’s boy. That’s all right, though. It will make you formidable.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IS"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IT">The boy felt both pleased and troubled by this. “He’ll—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7IU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7IV">“Oh, he’ll hang.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7J0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7J1">The boy nodded. “I want to see it.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7J2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7J3">The elder Deschain threw his head back and roared laughter. “Not as formidable as I thought . . . or perhaps just stupid.” He closed his mouth abruptly. An arm shot out and grabbed the boy’s upper arm painfully. Roland grimaced but didn’t flinch. His father peered at him steadily, and the boy looked back, although it was more difficult than hooding the hawk had been.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7J4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7J5">“All right,” he said, “thee may.” And turned abruptly to go.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7J6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7J7">“Father?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7J8"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7J9">“What?”</span></p>
<a aid="BE7JA"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JC">“Do you know who they were talking about? Do you know who the good man is?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JD"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JE">His father turned back and looked at him speculatively. “Yes. I think I do.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JG">“If you caught him,” Roland said in his thoughtful, near-plodding way, “no one else like Cook would have to be neck-popped.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JI">His father smiled thinly. “Perhaps not for a while. But in the end, someone always has to have his or her neck popped, as you so quaintly put it. The people demand it. Sooner or later, if there isn’t a turncoat, the people make one.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JK">“Yes,” Roland said, grasping the concept instantly—it was one he never forgot. “But if you got the good man—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JL"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JM">“No,” his father said flatly.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JN"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JO">“Why not? Why wouldn’t that end it?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JP"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JQ">For a moment his father seemed on the verge of saying why, but then shook his head. “We’ve talked enough for now, I think. Go out from me.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7JR"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7JS">He wanted to tell his father not to forget his promise when the time came for Hax to step through the trap, but he was sensitive to his father’s moods. He put his fist to his forehead, crossed one foot in front of the other, and bowed. Then he went out, closing the door quickly. He suspected that what his father wanted now was to fuck. He was aware that his mother and father did that, and he was reasonably well informed as to how it was done, but the mental picture that always condensed with the thought made him feel both uneasy and oddly guilty. Some years later, Susan would tell him the story of Oedipus, and he would absorb it in quiet thoughtfulness, thinking of the odd and bloody triangle formed by his father, his mother, and by Marten—known in some quarters as Farson, the good man. Or perhaps it was a quadrangle, if one wished to add himself.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7JT"></a>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE7JU"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE7JV"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE7K0"><span class="bold1" aid="BE7K1">XI</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE7K2"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE7K3"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7K4">Gallows Hill was on the Taunton Road, which was nicely poetic; Cuthbert might have appreciated this, but Roland did not. He did appreciate the splendidly ominous scaffold which climbed into the brilliantly blue sky, an angular silhouette which overhung the coach road.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7K5"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7K6">The two boys had been let out of Morning Exercises—Cort had read the notes from their fathers laboriously, lips moving, nodding here and there. When he finished with them, he had carefully put the papers away in his pocket. Even here in Gilead, paper was easily as valuable as gold. When these two sheets of it were safe, he’d looked up at the blue-violet dawn sky and nodded again.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7K7"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7K8">“Wait here,” he said, and went toward the leaning stone hut that served him as living quarters. He came back with a slice of rough, unleavened bread, broke it in two, and gave half to each.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7K9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KA">“When it’s over, each of you will put this beneath his shoes. Mind you do exactly as I say or I’ll clout you into next week.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KC">They had not understood until they arrived, riding double on Cuthbert’s gelding. They were the first, fully two hours ahead of anyone else and four hours before the hanging, so Gallows Hill stood deserted—except for the rooks and ravens. The birds were everywhere. They roosted noisily on the hard, jutting bar that overhung the trap—the armature of death. They sat in a row along the edge of the platform, they jostled for position on the stairs.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KD"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KE">“They leave the bodies,” Cuthbert muttered. “For the birds.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KG">“Let’s go up,” Roland said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KI">Cuthbert looked at him with something like horror. “Up <span class="italic" aid="BE7KJ">there?</span> Do you think—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KK"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KL">Roland cut him off with a gesture of his hands. “We’re <span class="italic" aid="BE7KM">years</span> early. No one will come.”</span></p>
<a aid="BE7KN"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KO"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KP">“All right.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KR">They walked slowly toward the gibbet, and the birds took wing, cawing and circling like a mob of angry dispossessed peasants. Their bodies were flat black against the pure dawnlight of the In-World sky.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KS"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KT">For the first time Roland felt the enormity of his responsibility in the matter; this wood was not noble, not part of the awesome machine of Civilization, but merely warped pine from the Forest o’ Barony, covered with splattered white bird droppings. It was splashed everywhere—stairs, railing, platform—and it stank.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7KU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7KV">The boy turned to Cuthbert with startled, terrified eyes and saw Cuthbert looking back at him with the same expression.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7L0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7L1">“I can’t,” Cuthbert whispered. “Ro’, I can’t watch it.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7L2"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7L3">Roland shook his head slowly. There was a lesson here, he realized, not a shining thing but something that was old and rusty and misshapen. It was why their fathers had let them come. And with his usual stubborn and inarticulate doggedness, Roland laid mental hands on whatever it was.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7L4"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7L5">“You can, Bert.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7L6"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7L7">“I won’t sleep tonight if I do.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7L8"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7L9">“Then you won’t,” Roland said, not seeing what that had to do with it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LA"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LB">Cuthbert suddenly seized Roland’s hand and looked at him with such mute agony that Roland’s own doubt came back, and he wished sickly that they had never gone to the west kitchen that night. His father had been right. Better not to know. Better every man, woman, and child in Taunton dead and stinking than this.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LC"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LD">But still. Still. Whatever the lesson was, rusty, whatever half-buried thing with sharp edges, he would not let it go or give up his grip on it.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LE"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LF">“Let’s not go up,” Cuthbert said. “We’ve seen everything.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LG"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LH">And Roland nodded reluctantly, feeling his grip on that <a aid="BE7LI"></a>thing—whatever it was—weaken. Cort, he knew, would have knocked them both sprawling and then forced them up to the platform step by cursing step . . . and sniffing fresh blood back up their noses and down their throats like salty jam as they went. Cort would probably have looped new hemp over the yardarm itself and put the noose around each of their necks in turn, would have made them stand on the trap to feel it; and Cort would have been ready to strike them again if either wept or lost control of his bladder. And Cort, of course, would have been right. For the first time in his life, Roland found himself hating his own childhood. He wished for the long boots of age.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LK">He deliberately pried a splinter from the railing and placed it in his breast pocket before turning away.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LL"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LM">“Why did you do that?” Cuthbert asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LN"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LO">He wished to answer something swaggering: <span class="italic" aid="BE7LP">Oh, the luck of the gallows . . . ,</span> but he only looked at Cuthbert and shook his head. “Just so I’ll have it,” he said. “Always have it.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7LQ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7LR">They walked away from the gallows, sat down, and waited. In an hour or so the first of the townfolk began to gather, mostly families who had come in broken-down wagons and beat-up buckas, carrying their breakfasts with them—hampers of cold pancakes folded over fillings of wild pokeberry jam. Roland felt his stomach growl hungrily and wondered again, with despair, where the honor and the nobility was. He had been taught of such things, and was now forced to wonder if they had been lies all along, or only treasures buried deep by the wise. He <span class="italic" aid="BE7LS">wanted</span> to believe that, but it seemed to him that Hax in his dirty whites, walking around and around his steaming, subterranean kitchen and yelling at the potboys, had more honor than this. He fingered the splinter from the gallows tree with sick bewilderment. Cuthbert lay beside him with his face drawn impassive.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7LT"></a>
<div class="calibre_5" aid="BE7LU"> </div><h3 class="calibre_8" aid="BE7LV"><span class="calibre9" aid="BE7M0"><span class="bold1" aid="BE7M1">XII</span></span></h3><div class="calibre_1" aid="BE7M2"> </div>
<p class="calibre_9" aid="BE7M3"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7M4">In the end it wasn’t such of a much, and Roland was glad. Hax was carried in an open cart, but only his huge girth gave him away; he had been blindfolded with a wide black cloth that hung down over his face. A few threw stones, but most merely continued with their breakfasts as they watched.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7M5"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7M6">A gunslinger whom the boy did not know well (he was glad his father had not drawn the black stone) led the fat cook carefully up the steps. Two Guards of the Watch had gone ahead and stood on either side of the trap. When Hax and the gunslinger reached the top, the gunslinger threw the noosed rope over the crosstree and then put it over the cook’s head, dropping the knot until it lay just below the left ear. The birds had all flown, but Roland knew they were waiting.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7M7"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7M8">“Do you wish to make confession?” the gunslinger asked.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7M9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MA">“I have nothing to confess,” Hax said. His words carried well, and his voice was oddly dignified in spite of the muffle of cloth which hung over his lips. The cloth ruffled slightly in the faint, pleasant breeze that had blown up. “I have not forgotten my father’s face; it has been with me through all.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MC">Roland glanced sharply at the crowd and was disturbed by what he saw there—a sense of sympathy? Perhaps admiration? He would ask his father. When traitors are called heroes (or heroes traitors, he supposed in his frowning way), dark times must have fallen. Dark times, indeed. He wished he understood better. His mind flashed to Cort and the bread Cort had given them. He felt contempt; the day was coming when Cort would serve him. Perhaps not Cuthbert; perhaps Bert would buckle under Cort’s steady fire and remain a page or a horseboy (or infinitely worse, a perfumed diplomat, dallying in receiving chambers or looking <a aid="BE7MD"></a>into bogus crystal balls with doddering kings and princes), but he would not. He knew it. He was for the open lands and long rides. That this seemed a <span class="italic" aid="BE7ME">good</span> fate was something he would marvel over later, in his solitude.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MG">“Roland?”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MH"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MI">“I’m here.” He took Cuthbert’s hand, and their fingers locked together like iron.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MJ"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MK">“Charge be capital murder and sedition,” the gunslinger said. “You have crossed the white, and I, Charles son of Charles, consign you ever to the black.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7ML"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MM">The crowd murmured, some in protest.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MN"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MO">“I never—”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MP"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MQ">“Tell your tale in the underworld, maggot,” said Charles of Charles, and yanked the lever with both yellow-gauntleted hands.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MR"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MS">The trap dropped. Hax plummeted through, still trying to talk. Roland never forgot that. The cook went <span class="italic" aid="BE7MT">still trying to talk.</span> And where did he finish the last sentence he would ever begin on earth? His words were ended by the sound an exploding pineknot makes on the hearth in the cold heart of a winter night.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7MU"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7MV">But on the whole he thought it not so much. The cook’s legs kicked out once in a wide Y; the crowd made a satisfied whistling noise; the Guards of the Watch dropped their military pose and began to gather things up negligently. Charles son of Charles walked back down the steps slowly, mounted his horse, and rode off, cutting roughly through one gaggle of picnickers, quirting a few of the slowcoaches, making them scurry.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7N0"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7N1">The crowd dispersed rapidly after that, and in forty minutes the two boys were left alone on the small hill they had chosen. The birds were returning to examine their new prize. One lit on Hax’s shoulder and sat there chummily, darting its beak at the bright and shiny hoop Hax had always worn in his right ear.</span></p>
<a aid="BE7N2"></a>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7N3"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7N4">“It doesn’t look like him at all,” Cuthbert said.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7N5"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7N6">“Oh yes, it does,” Roland said confidently as they walked toward the gallows, the bread in their hands. Bert looked abashed.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7N7"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7N8">They paused beneath the crosstree, looking up at the dangling, twisting body. Cuthbert reached up and touched one hairy ankle, defiantly. The body started on a new, twisting arc.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7N9"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7NA">Then, rapidly, they broke the bread and spread the rough chunks beneath the dangling feet. Roland looked back just once as they rode away. Now there were thousands of birds. The bread—he grasped this only dimly—was symbolic, then.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7NB"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7NC">“It was good,” Cuthbert said suddenly. “It . . . I . . . I liked it. I did.”</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7ND"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7NE">Roland was not shocked by this, although he had not particularly cared for the scene. But he thought he could perhaps understand what Bert was saying. Perhaps he’d not finish as a diplomat after all, jokes and easy line of talk or not.</span></p>
<p class="calibre_10" aid="BE7NF"><span class="calibre3" aid="BE7NG">“I don’t know about that,” he said, “but it was something. It surely was.”</span></p>
